Cause of Disagreements in Kalam: Neglect of Arabic
Explains that theological disagreements stem from abandoning Arabic for foreign logic and philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the argument that theological disagreements in kalam arose from neglecting the Arabic language and adopting foreign (Greek) thought, and how this is supported by classical scholars and the Quran.

Core Idea
Neglecting Arabic for Greek logic is the root of theological confusion.

This passage tackles a fundamental methodological issue in kalam: why did theologians disagree so much? The author argues that the problem is not intellectual deficiency but a linguistic and cultural shift away from the Quranic language toward Greek philosophy. Three major scholars—al-Shafi'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, and al-Suyuti—support this claim, and a Quranic verse reinforces the necessity of using the people's language for clear communication. By the end of this lesson, you will understand this core diagnosis of the cause of theological error.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing it. Then read the literal translation. Let the author's argument and the supporting quotes come through before interpreting.
Note the key terms: العجمة (foreignness), لسان العرب (language of the Arabs), أرسطاطاليس (Aristotle).
2
Identify the three scholars and their statements
Find the quotes from Imam al-Shafi'i, al-Hasan al-Basri, and Imam al-Suyuti. Understand what each one says about the cause of ignorance and disagreement.
Al-Shafi'i blames abandoning Arabic for Aristotle's language. Al-Hasan al-Basri blames 'ajam (foreignness). Al-Suyuti accuses deviants of interpreting revelation with Greek logic.
3
Connect the Quranic verse to the argument
Read the verse from Surah Ibrahim (14:4): Allah sends messengers with the language of their people for clear explanation. Reflect on how this supports the claim that using a foreign framework distorts the message.
The verse implies that using a non-Arabic framework to understand the Arabic Qur'an leads to misinterpretation.
4
Reflect on the implication
Consider the consequence: if theological disagreements stem from methodological corruption (adopting Greek logic), then proper methodology requires returning to Arabic language and Arab-Islamic heritage.
This is a methodological critique of those who imported Greek philosophy into Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أما لماذا اختلف رجال الكلام وتباينت أقوالهم، فإن ذلك يرجع إلى العجمة التي أصابت المسلمين بسبب عكوفهم على الفكر الأجنبي، وإهمالهم لغة القرآن، وأساليب العربية . يقول الإمام الشافعي رضي الله عنه : «ما جهل الناس، ولا اختلفوا إلا لتركهم لسان العرب، وميلهم إلى لسان أرسطاطا ليس» (۲) وأخرج الإمام البخاري في تاريخه الكبير، عن الحسن البصري رضي الله عنهما قال: «إنما أهلكتهم العجمة». ويرى الإمام السيوطي رحمه الله أن هؤلاء المارقين، أخذوا يؤولون القرآن والحديث النبوي على مصطلح لسان يونان، ومنطق أرسطاطاليس. وما نزل القرآن إلا بلغة العرب وببيان العرب قال تعالى : ومَا أَرْسَلْنا من رسول إِلَّا بِلِسَانِ قَوْمِهِ لِيُبَيِّنَ لَهُمْ ) (۳).
As for why the men of kalam differed and their statements diverged, that is due to the non-Arabism ('ajam) that afflicted Muslims because of their devotion to foreign thought and their neglect of the language of the Qur'an and the styles of Arabic. Imam al-Shafi'i, may Allah be pleased with him, said: 'People did not become ignorant and did not differ except by abandoning the tongue of the Arabs and inclining to the tongue of Aristotle.' And Imam al-Bukhari narrated in his Great History from al-Hasan al-Basri, may Allah be pleased with both of them, who said: 'Non-Arabism destroyed them.' And Imam al-Suyuti, may Allah have mercy on him, holds that these deviants began to interpret the Qur'an and prophetic hadith according to the terminology of the Greek tongue and the logic of Aristotle. And the Qur'an was only sent down in the language of the Arabs and in the Arabic explanation. Allah Most High says: 'And We sent not a Messenger except with the language of his people, that he might make (the message) clear for them' (Surah Ibrahim, verse 4).
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage is an explanatory statement about the root cause of disagreements among mutakallimun (theologians). The author argues that the differences and divergent opinions in kalam are due to 'ajam (non-Arabism or foreign influence) that afflicted Muslims when they occupied themselves with foreign thought and neglected the Arabic language of the Qur'an and its rhetorical styles. This is supported by three authoritative citations. First, Imam al-Shafi'i states that ignorance and disagreement arise only from abandoning the language of the Arabs and inclining to the language of Aristotle (i.e., Greek philosophy). Second, al-Bukhari quotes al-Hasan al-Basri saying that 'ajam destroyed them. Third, al-Suyuti claims that the deviants interpreted the Qur'an and hadith according to Greek terminology and Aristotelian logic. Finally, the author cites the Quranic verse that every messenger was sent with the language of his people to make the message clear, implying that using a foreign framework distorts the divine message. The overall argument is that theological error originates from linguistic and methodological departure from the Arab-Islamic heritage.

Key Terms
العجمة
لسان العرب
أرسطاطاليس
تأويل
المنطق

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That the issue was merely academic disagreement, rather than a fundamental methodological corruption from adopting non-Islamic sources.
Correction: The passage shows that the cause is not just differing opinions but a deviation in methodology: abandoning the Arabic language and adopting Greek logic and philosophy.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what does Imam al-Shafi'i identify as the cause of ignorance and disagreement?
A) Lack of intelligence
B) Abandoning the language of the Arabs and adopting the language of Aristotle
C) Too much focus on the Qur'an
D) Following the Companions

Key Takeaway
Theological disagreements arose because Muslims adopted Greek philosophy and neglected the Arabic language, leading to misinterpretation of revelation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever felt a tension between your intellectual training (e.g., in modern science or philosophy) and the straightforward reading of the Quran? Which framework do you subconsciously trust more?
?

What does your first instinct reveal about your reliance on Arabic or on foreign frameworks?
?
The scholars quoted in this lesson warn that adopting non-Arabic methods leads to destruction. Is there a specific method or presupposition you have inherited from a non-Islamic source that you have not examined? What would it cost you to set it aside and return to the Arabic of the Revelation?

Deviation from Sharia and the Importance of Arabic
An anecdote illustrating theological error from linguistic ignorance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how a Mu'tazilite scholar erred due to ignorance of Arabic linguistic nuances and why mastering the language of revelation is essential for sound theological reasoning.

Core Idea
Ignorance of Arabic led the Mu'tazila to confuse threat with promise, creating a false theological obligation.

This lesson presents a famous anecdote from the early Islamic scholarly tradition. A Mu'tazilite debater, Amr bin Ubayd, argues from a Qur'anic verse of threat that God must punish the grave sinner. The philologist Amr bin al-'Ala' corrects him by pointing out a subtle but crucial distinction in Arabic between threat and promise. The lesson underscores that sound theology requires not only scriptural knowledge but also linguistic precision.

Guided Path
1
Read the story and identify the characters
Read the Arabic passage and translation. Notice the two scholars and their positions. Amr bin Ubayd was a Mu'tazilite; Amr bin al-'Ala' was a grammarian.
The story is narrated by Imam al-Bayhaqi to support the importance of adhering to the language of the Shari'a.
2
Understand the linguistic distinction
Focus on Amr bin al-'Ala''s correction: 'al-wa'id' (threat) is distinct from 'al-i'ad' (promise of punishment). The line of poetry shows that threats can be broken while promises must be kept.
The Arabic word 'wa'id' carries a nuance of intimidation that may or may not be carried out.
3
Reflect on the broader lesson
Consider why the author uses this story to emphasize deviation from Sharia and its language leads to ignorance. The Mu'tazilites' error stemmed from a linguistic misunderstanding, leading to a flawed theology.
This underscores that theological reasoning must be grounded in the precise meanings of the revealed text.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فمن عدل عن الشرع جهل وضل . لسان الشرع إلى لسان غيره وخرج عن الوارد من نصوص ويؤيد ما قاله الأئمة الأجلاء ما أخرجه الإمام البيهقي قال: جاء عمرو بن عبيد إلى عمرو بن العلاء يناظره في وجوب عذاب الفاسق . فقال له : يا أبا عمرو الله يخلف وعده؟ فقال : لن يخلف الله وعده . فقال عمرو : فقد قال : وذكر آية وعيد . فقال أبو عبيد من العجمة أتيت الوعيد غير الإيعاد ثم أنشد : وإني وإن أوعدته أو وعـدتـه لمخلف إيعادي ومنجز موعدي (١) (۱) صون المنطق والكلام عن فن المنطق للسيوطي جـا ص ٢٢ .
For whoever turns away from the Shari'a is ignorant and misguided. [He prefers] the language of the Shari'a to another language and deviates from the transmitted texts. And what supports what the noble imams said is what Imam al-Bayhaqi narrated: He said: 'Amr bin Ubayd came to Amr bin al-'Ala' debating with him about the obligation of punishment for the grave sinner.' So he said to him: 'O Abu Amr, does God break His promise?' He said: 'God never breaks His promise.' So Amr said: 'He has said,' and mentioned a verse of threat. So Abu 'Amr [bin al-'Ala'] replied: 'You have come from non-Arabic; threat (al-wa'id) is other than promise of punishment (al-i'ad).' Then he recited: 'And verily, if I threaten him or promise him, I break my [promise of] punishment and fulfill my promise.' (1) (1) Sawm al-Mantiq wa al-Kalam 'an Fann al-Mantiq by al-Suyuti, volume, page 22.
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage presents an anecdote supporting the claim that deviation from the Shari'a and its language leads to ignorance. The author first states a general principle: whoever turns away from the Shari'a is ignorant and misguided, and prefers another language to the language of the Shari'a, thus deviating from transmitted texts. Then he introduces a story narrated by al-Bayhaqi to support what the noble imams said. In the story, Amr bin Ubayd, a Mu'tazilite scholar, debates Amr bin al-'Ala', a philologist, about the obligation of punishment for the grave sinner (the fasiq). Amr bin Ubayd argues from a Qur'anic verse of threat (wa'id) that God must punish the sinner, assuming that God's threat is a binding promise. Amr bin al-'Ala' corrects him by pointing out a linguistic subtlety: in Arabic, 'wa'id' (threat) is not the same as 'i'ad (promise of punishment). A threat may be carried out or not, whereas a promise must be fulfilled. He supports this with a line of poetry showing that the speaker distinguishes between breaking a threat and fulfilling a promise. The implication is that those who engage in theological reasoning without proper linguistic understanding—like the Mu'tazila—fall into error. The footnote cites al-Suyuti's work on logic and speech sciences.

Key Terms
وعيد
إيعاد
عذاب الفاسق
من العجمة
لسان الشرع

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That wa'id (threat) and i'ad (promise of punishment) are synonyms, so God's threat of punishment must necessarily be fulfilled, making punishment obligatory for the grave sinner.
Correction: In Arabic, wa'id (threat) is distinct from i'ad (promise of punishment). A threat may be broken or forgiven, whereas a promise must be kept. Thus, God's threats are not binding in the same way as promises.

Checkpoint
What linguistic distinction does Amr bin al-'Ala' make in the story?
A) Threat and promise are the same in Arabic.
B) 'Wa'id' (threat) is distinct from 'i'ad' (promise of punishment); a threat need not be fulfilled.
C) The word 'wa'id' does not exist in Arabic.
D) Punishment for the fasiq is obligatory based on the verse.

Key Takeaway
Sound theological reasoning requires mastery of the Arabic language, as even subtle linguistic distinctions can lead to major doctrinal errors.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever relied on a superficial understanding of a religious text because you assumed you knew the language? How might that error have affected your beliefs or actions?
?
The Mu'tazilite built a whole theology on a linguistic mistake. What areas of your own faith might be built on assumptions you have not deeply examined?
?
If you were to master Arabic to truly understand the revelation, what sacrifices would you need to make? Are you willing to take that step starting today?

The Salaf's Consensus and al-Karabisi's Repentance
Author cites Salaf's consensus and a repentance story against kalam.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Salaf's consensus and the repentance of a leading kalam scholar serve as historical evidence against engaging in ilm al-kalam.

Core Idea
Even the most learned in kalam repudiated it on their deathbed.

This lesson presents historical testimony from the early Muslim community. The righteous predecessors (Salaf) and their followers are reported to have nearly unanimously considered ilm al-kalam a harmful innovation. To emphasize this point, the author recounts a powerful story: a renowned Mu'tazilī scholar, al-Walīd ibn Abān al-Karābīsī, on his deathbed, despite his unmatched expertise, advised his sons to abandon kalam. This narrative aims to show that even those who excelled in dialectical theology ultimately recognized its danger.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation once to get the overall flow. Notice the two main parts: a general claim about the Salaf and a specific story about al-Karabisi.
2
Understand the claim about the Salaf
Identify what is being claimed about the Salaf and the Tābiʿūn regarding ilm al-kalam. What reason is given for their consensus?
The claim is that they almost unanimously considered kalam a bid'ah that divided the Muslims.
3
Analyze the story of al-Karabisi
Look at the dialogue between al-Karabisi and his sons. Why does he ask them those specific questions? What is the implication of his advice?
He establishes his credibility and then gives a final counsel—presumably to abandon kalam.
4
Reflect on the lesson's significance
Consider why the author includes this story. How does it reinforce the earlier claim? What does it teach about the attitude of even expert practitioners toward kalam at the end of their lives?
The story shows that personal expertise does not validate the field; rather, it can lead to eventual repentance.

(۱) هو الوليد بن أبان الكرابيسي : معتزلي من علماء الكلام من أهل البصرة له مقالات في تقوية مذهب الاعتزال. نسبته إلى بيع الكرابيس، وهو قد توفي عام ٢١٤هـ
The righteous predecessors (al-salaf al-ṣāliḥ) and the followers (al-tābiʿūn) after them almost unanimously agree that ilm al-kalam is an innovation (bidʿah), and an innovation that had the greatest impact in dividing the Muslims and dispersing their unity. Scholars who were deceived by the glitter of argumentation and whose minds were captivated by its false brilliance eventually, even in their final days, repented from it and advised others to keep away from it and not to approach its source. It is narrated from Aḥmad ibn Sinān that he said: Al-Walīd ibn Abān al-Karābīsī was my maternal uncle. When death approached him, he said to his sons: 'Do you know anyone more knowledgeable in kalam than me?' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Do you suspect me?' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Then I advise you; will you accept?' They said: 'Yes.' (1) He is al-Walīd ibn Abān al-Karābīsī: a Muʿtazilī scholar of kalam from Basra, who had treatises strengthening the Muʿtazilī school. His nisbah is to the selling of karābīs (cloth), and he died in the year 214 AH.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage provides historical evidence for the position that ilm al-kalam is a blameworthy innovation. It begins by asserting that the righteous Salaf and their successors (the Tābiʿūn) were nearly unanimous in considering kalam a bidʿah that caused division and disunity among Muslims. The author then notes that even scholars who were initially attracted to the deceptive brilliance of dialectical argumentation eventually repented in their later years and warned others to stay away from it. To illustrate this, the author relates a specific story: Aḥmad ibn Sinān narrates that his maternal uncle, al-Walīd ibn Abān al-Karābīsī—a prominent Muʿtazilī scholar—on his deathbed asked his sons if they knew anyone more learned in kalam than him. They answered no. He then asked if they suspected him of lying; they said no. So he gave them an advice, which they agreed to accept. The implication is that he advised them to abandon kalam. The footnote identifies al-Karābīsī as a Muʿtazilī theologian who wrote works supporting Muʿtazilism and died in 214 AH.

Correction: The story of al-Karābīsī shows that despite being the most knowledgeable in kalam, he repented on his deathbed and advised his sons to abandon it, indicating that expertise does not justify the practice.

D) He declared that kalam was the highest form of knowledge.

Key Takeaway
The Salaf's consensus and the deathbed repentance of a leading kalam scholar provide strong historical evidence against the pursuit of ilm al-kalam.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

The Salaf nearly unanimously considered kalam a bid'ah that divides Muslims. If you have ever engaged in theological debates that created distance between you and a brother, will you seek repentance and change your approach to learning and teaching deen?
?
Al-Karabisi, the most knowledgeable in his field, ended his life with regret and advised his own sons to abandon kalam. What if your highest achievements in knowledge become a source of regret on your deathbed? What are you building your legacy upon—argumentation or sincere obedience?

The Salaf's Condemnation of Kalam and Repentance Testimonies
Presents the Salaf's consensus against kalam and two repentance testimonies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the near-consensus among the Salaf that ilm al-kalam is a blameworthy innovation, and how leading theologians like al-Karabisi and al-Juwayni repented from it.

Core Idea
Even masters of kalam renounced it on their deathbeds, affirming the simpler faith of the Salaf.

This passage from al-Maqasid presents a powerful historical argument against engaging in dialectical theology (kalam). The author begins by stating that the righteous predecessors (Salaf) and their successors almost unanimously considered kalam a divisive innovation. Then, he provides two striking testimonies: a leading Mu'tazili theologian and a major Ash'ari imam both repented from kalam and urged others to follow the People of Hadith or the simple faith of old women. The passage then begins to cite the four Imams, starting with Imam Malik, but is cut off.

Guided Path
1
Understand the Salaf's near-consensus
Read the opening statement: the Salaf agreed that kalam is an innovation that caused division. Note that this is presented as a historical fact, not a personal opinion.
2
Examine al-Karabisi's deathbed advice
Al-Walid ibn Aban al-Karabisi, a Mu'tazili scholar, on his deathbed told his sons to follow the People of Hadith, admitting truth was with them.
Consider the irony: a theologian of his caliber renounces his own field.
3
Analyze al-Juwayni's repentance
Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, a giant of Ash'ari kalam, renounced his life's work and urged his students to avoid kalam, advocating the religion of old women (din al-ajayiz).
Reflect on his statement 'woe to Ibn al-Juwayni'.
4
Introduce the four Imams' views (incomplete)
The passage begins to quote Imam Malik defining innovation as speaking about Allah's attributes beyond the Salaf. This part is incomplete but sets the stage for the next section.
The text ends mid-sentence on page 35.

موقف السلف من علم الكلام
ويكاد السلف الصالح والتابعون من بعدهم يتفقون على أن علم الكلام بدعة، وبدعة كان لها أكبر الأثر في تفريق المسلمين وتشتيت وحدتهم . العلماء الذين خدعهم بريق الجدل وأخذ بلبهم بهرجه الزائف عادوا حتى في أخريات حياتهم يتوبون منه ، ويوصون غيرهم بالبعد عنه، وعدم الاقتراب من منهله. يروى عن أحمد بن سنان قال : كان الوليد بن أبان الكرابيسي (١) خالي، فلما حضرته الوفاة قال لبنيه : تعلمون أحداً أعلم بالكلام مني ..؟ قالوا : لا . قال : فتتهمونني . قالوا : لا . قال : فإني أوصيكم أتقبلون ..؟ قالوا : نعم . قال : عليكم بما عليه أصحاب الحديث، فإني رأيت الحق معهم .

وكان أبو المعالي الجويني يقول : لقد جلت أهل الإسلام جولة، وعلومهم وركبت البحر الأعظم وغصت في الذي نهوا عنه كل ذلك في طلب الحق، وهرباً من التقليد، والآن فقد رجعت عن الكل إلى كلمة الحق . عليكم بدين العجائز فإن لم يدركني الحق بلطيف بره، فأموت على دين العجائز ، ويختم عاقبة أمري عند الرحيل بكلمة الإخلاص، فالويل لابن الجويني . وكان يقول لأصحابه : يا أصحابنا لا تشتغلوا بالكلام، فلو عرفت أن الكلام يبلغ بي ما بلغ ما تشاغلت به (١) . قال هذا رجل عاش حياته في مجادلة الخصوم ومناقشة العلماء ووضع كتابين على نمط منهج علماء : الكلام هما الإرشاد (٢)، والشامل في أصول الدين (٣).

ويطيب لي أن نستعرض آراء الأئمة الأربعة في علم الكلام . فالإمام مالك رضي الله عنه صاحب كتاب الموطأ يوصي أصحابه بقوله : إياكم والبدع قيل : يا أبا عبد الله، ومالبدع ..؟ قال : أهل البدع الذين يتكلمون في أسماء الله وصفاته، وكلامه وعلمه، وقدرته، ولا يسكتون عما سكب عنه الصحابة والتابعون لهم بإحسان» (٤) . ويروي ابن عبد البر، المتوفى سنة ٤٦٣ - ١٠٧٠م في كتاب (مختصر جامع بيان العلم وفضله) أن الإمام مالكاً كان يقول :
The Stance of the Salaf Regarding Ilm al-Kalam
Almost all the righteous Salaf (predecessors) and the successors (tabi'un) after them agree that ilm al-kalam is an innovation (bid'a), and an innovation that had the greatest effect in dividing the Muslims and dispersing their unity. The scholars who were deceived by the glitter of debate and whose hearts were captivated by its false splendor, even in the last days of their lives, they repented from it and advised others to keep away from it and not to approach its source. It is narrated from Ahmad ibn Sinan who said: Al-Walid ibn Aban al-Karabisi (1) was my maternal uncle. When death approached him, he said to his sons: 'Do you know anyone more knowledgeable in kalam than me?' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Do you suspect me?' They said: 'No.' He said: 'Then I advise you; will you accept?' They said: 'Yes.' He said: 'You must adhere to what the People of Hadith (ashab al-hadith) are upon, for I have seen the truth with them.'

And Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni used to say: 'I have traversed the people of Islam thoroughly, and their sciences, and I rode the great sea and dived into that which they forbade — all of that in search of truth and fleeing from blind imitation. Now I have returned from all of that to the word of truth. You must adhere to the religion of the old women (din al-ajayiz). If the truth does not reach me through His subtle proof, I will die upon the religion of the old women, and the end of my affair at departure will be sealed with the word of sincerity. So woe to Ibn al-Juwayni.' And he used to say to his companions: 'O our companions, do not occupy yourselves with kalam! For if I had known that kalam would lead me to what it has led me, I would not have occupied myself with it.' (1) He [the author] said: This man spent his life disputing opponents and debating scholars, and wrote two books following the methodology of the scholars of kalam: they are al-Irshad (2) and al-Shamil fi Usul al-Din (3).

And I would like to review the views of the four imams regarding ilm al-kalam. So Imam Malik — may Allah be pleased with him — the author of al-Muwatta', counseled his companions saying: 'Beware of innovations (bida').' It was said: 'O Abu Abd Allah, what are innovations?' He said: 'The people of innovation are those who speak about Allah's names and attributes, His speech, His knowledge, and His power, and they do not remain silent about what the Companions and the Followers who followed them in goodness were silent about.' (4) And Ibn Abd al-Barr, who died in 463 AH / 1070 CE, narrates in his book Mukhtasar Jami' Bayan al-Ilm wa Fadlihi that Imam Malik used to say:
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage presents the stance of the early Muslim community (Salaf) towards ilm al-kalam (dialectical theology). The author begins by stating a near-consensus among the righteous Salaf and their successors that kalam is an innovation (bid'a) that caused division among Muslims. He then provides two testimonies of scholars who repented from engaging in kalam at the end of their lives. First, the story of al-Walid ibn Aban al-Karabisi, a Mu'tazili theologian, who on his deathbed advised his sons to follow the People of Hadith, declaring that he saw truth with them. Second, the testimony of Abu al-Ma'ali al-Juwayni, a prominent Ash'ari theologian, who also renounced kalam, urged adherence to the simple faith of the elderly (din al-ajayiz), and warned his companions not to occupy themselves with kalam. The author notes that al-Juwayni authored major kalam works (al-Irshad and al-Shamil) yet eventually repented. The passage then begins to review the views of the four Imams, starting with Imam Malik, who defined innovations as speaking about Allah's names and attributes beyond what the Companions and early Followers discussed. The passage ends mid-sentence with Ibn Abd al-Barr's narration that Imam Malik used to say something further, which continues on the next page.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think that ilm al-kalam was universally accepted among early Muslims as a legitimate discipline.
Correction: This passage shows strong condemnation from the Salaf and even repentance from leading practitioners like al-Karabisi and al-Juwayni, indicating it was widely viewed as a harmful innovation.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The Salaf and even leading mutakallimun ultimately warned against kalam, pointing to a simple, text-based faith as the safer path.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Al-Juwayni spent his life in kalam and wrote major works, yet on his deathbed he wished he had not. Is there any area of religious knowledge you pursue for the sake of argument or pride rather than for Allah? How can you check this against the example of Juwayni?
?
The Salaf agreed that kalam is a bid'a that divides. Do you feel any attachment to divisive theological debates? What would it mean for you to adopt the 'religion of old women' in your current study?
?
Imam Malik defined innovators as those who speak about Allah's names and attributes beyond what the Salaf did. In your own heart, are you content with what Allah has revealed about Himself, or do you find yourself desiring to 'go deeper' in a way that leads to unnecessary speculation?

Abu Hanifa's Advice: Stick to the Way of the Salaf
Abu Hanifa warns against kalam on atoms and bodies as philosophy.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Abu Hanifa's critical view of engaging in kalam about atoms and bodies and his directive to adhere to the transmitted narrations and the method of the Salaf.

Core Idea
Abu Hanifa warned that kalam about atoms is philosophy, not theology.

This lesson presents a famous report in which Abu Hanifa is asked about the newly introduced discourse on accidents and bodies. His response sharply distinguishes between legitimate transmitted knowledge and speculative philosophy, guiding the student to prioritize the narrations of the Salaf and avoid innovation.

Guided Path
1
Read the anecdote carefully
Read the Arabic passage once for overall meaning, then read the translation. Notice the question and the directness of Abu Hanifa's answer.
The passage is very brief but packs a strong methodological statement.
2
Identify what Abu Hanifa condemns
Abu Hanifa labels discourse about accidents and bodies as 'the sayings of the philosophers.' Understand why he equates this kalam with philosophy.
3
Identify what he endorses
He advises sticking to 'al-athar' (the transmitted reports) and the 'way of the Salaf' (early pious predecessors). He also warns against every innovation (muhdatha) calling it bid'a.
This sets a clear methodological boundary.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويذكر الهروي عن أبي المظفر للسمعاني ; قال : قلت لأبي حنيفة : ما تقول فيما أحدث الناس من الكلام في الأعراض والأجسام ..؟ فقال : مقالات الفلاسفة . . قلت : نعم . قال : عمليك بالأثر وطريقة السلف، وإياك وكل محدثة فإنها بدعة
And al-Harawi mentions from Abu al-Muzaffar al-Sam'ani: He said: I said to Abu Hanifa: 'What do you say about what people have innovated of discourse concerning accidents and bodies?' He said: 'The sayings of the philosophers.' I said: 'Yes.' He said: 'Upon you [stick] to the narration (al-athar) and the way of the Salaf, and beware of every innovation, for it is a bid'a.'
Source pages p. 36

Commentary
This passage is a report of a question put to Imam Abu Hanifa about the newly introduced theological discourse concerning accidents (a'rad) and bodies (ajsam) – topics central to kalam. Abu Hanifa responds by categorizing such discussions as 'the sayings of the philosophers' (maqalat al-falasifa), implying they are borrowed from Greek philosophy. He then advises the questioner to adhere to the transmitted reports (athar) and the methodology of the early pious predecessors (Salaf), and to avoid every innovation (muhdatha) because it is a religious innovation (bid'a). This reinforces his rejection of speculative kalam.

Key Terms
الأعراض
الأجسام
الفلسفة
الأثر
السلف
بدعة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think Abu Hanifa rejected all forms of theological reasoning and rational discussion.
Correction: He specifically rejected kalam about atoms and bodies (accidents and substances) as philosophical, but he did not reject all rational argumentation; he endorsed following the Athar and Salaf.

Checkpoint
How did Abu Hanifa categorize discourse about accidents and bodies?
A) As necessary for faith
B) As the sayings of the philosophers
C) As permissible if sincere
D) As part of the Salaf's way

Key Takeaway
Abu Hanifa advised avoiding speculative kalam about atoms and bodies and instead following the transmitted way of the Salaf.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You just read that Abu Hanifa labeled discussions about 'accidents and bodies' as 'the sayings of the philosophers' and commanded sticking to 'al-athar wa tareeq al-salaf'. Now examine your own heart: Are there aspects of your faith—your beliefs about Allah, destiny, or the unseen—that you have shaped more by human reasoning, philosophical assumptions, or cultural ideas than by what was transmitted from the Prophet and the Salaf? Identify one such belief right now. What would it cost you to surrender it and submit purely to the way of the Salaf? Will you make that surrender today?
?
Abu Hanifa warned that every 'muhdatha' (innovation) is a 'bid'a' that leads astray. Look at your daily religious practice—your worship, your gatherings, your learning. What is one practice or emphasis you engage in that has no clear precedent from the Salaf? Even if it feels good or seems wise, are you willing to abandon it outright for the sake of following the narrated path? If not, what is holding you back from trusting the Salaf's way more than your own reasoning?

Ghazali's View on Kalam: Past Luxury, Present Danger
Ghazali excuses past kalam debates, warns of current danger, and introduces a question.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand Muhammad Ghazali's historical justification for past kalam debates and his implied challenge for Muslims today.

Core Idea
Kalam debates were once intellectual luxury; now they threaten unity.

Shaykh Muhammad al-Ghazali offers a striking perspective: he excuses past scholars for their deep involvement in kalam because the Islamic state was strong and they had leisure. He then warns that those debates have outlived their scholars and now endanger the umma's unity. This sets up an urgent question for Muslims today.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's rhythm come through.
Notice the polite phrase 'May God forgive our forefathers.'
2
Identify Ghazali's two-part argument
Look for the historical excuse (past) and the present warning. Underline phrases that show why earlier engagement was harmless and why it's dangerous now.
3
Reflect on 'intellectual luxury'
What does 'الترف العقلي' mean? Why would a strong state afford this luxury? Consider the contrast with jihad.
Ghazali calls kalam a 'dangerous field' even while excusing it.
4
Consider the transition question
The passage ends by introducing a third question. Why does Ghazali ask this now? What might his answer be? Think about the historical shift from strength to vulnerability.
This question is left unanswered – it invites your reflection.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يقول الشيخ محمد الغزالي : وعفا الله أجدادنا فقد أولعوا بذلك، وأعانهم عليه أن الدولة الإسلامية كانت سيدة العالم . فلا بأس على رجالها أن يشتغلوا بالترف العقلي، وأن يحولوا فراغهم من الجهاد في سبيل الله إلى جهاد في هذا الميدان الخطر، فانشغلوا بأنفسهم عن أعدائهم، ثم ذهب الرجال وبقي الجدال بقي إلى اليوم يهدد وحدة الأمة، ويهز كيانها . ونصل بذلك إلى السؤال الثالث: هل المسلمون الآن بحاجة إلى
Shaykh Muhammad al-Ghazali says: 'May God forgive our forefathers, for they were enamored with that, and the fact that the Islamic state was the master of the world aided them in that. So there is no harm in its men occupying themselves with intellectual luxury, and turning their leisure from jihad in the path of God into jihad in this dangerous field. So they became preoccupied with themselves instead of their enemies. Then the men passed away, and the argument remained – it remains to this day threatening the unity of the community and shaking its foundation.' And we thus arrive at the third question: Are Muslims today in need of...
Source pages p. 41

Commentary
This passage presents a modern perspective from Shaykh Muhammad al-Ghazali, who offers a historical justification for past kalam debates. He uses the phrase 'عفا الله أجدادنا' (May God forgive our forefathers) and explains that they engaged in such intellectual debates because the Islamic state was supreme and dominant, affording them leisure. He characterizes kalam as 'intellectual luxury' and a 'dangerous field'. He notes that they turned their time away from jihad against enemies to this internal disputation. After the scholars died, the arguments persisted and now threaten the umma's unity. The author then transitions to a third question: whether Muslims today need kalam, leaving it open-ended. The passage thus serves as both a critique and a partial excuse, setting up a contemporary inquiry.

Key Terms

الترف العقلي
الجدال
وحدة الأمة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Ghazali completely condemns all kalam debates.
Correction: He actually excuses past debates due to the strength of the Islamic state but warns that their remnants now threaten unity.

Checkpoint

A) The weakness of their enemies
B) The dominance of the Islamic state
C) Their ignorance of the Quran
D) The command of the caliphs

Key Takeaway
Ghazali excuses past kalam as intellectual luxury but warns it endangers umma unity today.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Think of a religious debate you have engaged in or witnessed recently. Was your primary concern being correct, or preserving unity? How does Ghazali's warning about debates that 'remain to this day threatening the unity of the community' challenge your attitude? What concrete step can you take to prioritize unity over winning an argument?
?
Ghazali says the early scholars turned their leisure from physical jihad into intellectual luxury. What do you fill your leisure time with? Is it drawing you closer to God and the community, or is it a form of intellectual luxury that distracts you from more urgent responsibilities? What would it take to redirect that time towards what truly matters?
?
The passage notes that 'the men passed away, and the argument remained'. Are there any arguments, issues, or grievances from the past that you are still holding onto—perhaps inherited from teachers or family—that are now harming your relationships or your own spiritual peace? Can you identify one such argument and commit to releasing it for the sake of unity?

The Urgent Need for Kalam in Modern Times
A polemical introduction demonstrating the necessity of kalam against modern heresies.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the science of kalam remains essential for refuting modern atheistic and heretical movements, and how classical heresies have evolved into contemporary challenges.

Core Idea
Kalam is the necessary weapon to defend faith against evolving heresies.

This passage from a modern commentary on Sharh al-Maqasid sets the stage for studying kalam by posing a pressing question: Do Muslims still need kalam? The author argues vehemently that yes, we do, because new forms of heresy—from modern Jahmiyyah to atheistic existentialism—require a sophisticated theological response. As you read, notice how the author connects ancient heresies to their modern counterparts, underscoring that kalam is not a relic but a living science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once and then its English translation. Pay attention to the rhetorical questions that frame the argument.
The author uses constant questioning to urge reflection.
2
Identify the old vs. new heresies
The passage pairs each ancient heresy (Jahmiyyah, Batiniyyah) with a modern version (Orientalism, nihilism). List these pairs in your notebook.
This shows that kalam's principles remain relevant yet need updating.
3
Reflect on the necessity
Answer the author's implicit question: Why is kalam still needed? Connect his examples to contemporary debates about scripture, reason, and atheism.
The passage ends with a call to action for scholars of 'Aqeedah.

And if the answer is in the negative, that Muslims now are not in need of it, then how do we repel the tyranny of the Jahmiyyah of the twentieth century? The first Jahmiyyah used to say: The Qur'an is the speech of God, created. As for the modern Jahmiyyah represented in Orientalism and its followers, and evangelism and its tails, they say the humanity of the Qur'an — that is, the Qur'an is manufactured and authored. Muhammad compiled it, and it is the mirror of Mecca to a specific horizon of life, the horizon of life in the Arabian Peninsula and in a special aspect (1). How do we debate the destructive modern schools? And what is our means in refuting the 'Nominalist' school, whose followers - may God fight them - declare that the word 'Allah' has no referent? And how do we debate the proponents of the 'Empiricist' school, which expresses its origins from Ludwig Feuerbach, a German philosopher, in his saying: 'God was my first idea, and reason was my second idea, and man in his real environment is my third and final idea' (2). How do we prevent our society from listening to these schools that seep into it through television at times, and radio at other times? The followers of the 'Rationalist' school call this age the age of enlightenment or the age of man, and the German Wolff interprets enlightenment in his saying: 'Excluding religion from the domain of guidance, and replacing it with reason.' The reason that has dispensed with the gods of myth and the church. The ancient Batiniyyah used to say: God is transcendent above similarity to creatures; if He were existent, He would resemble existents; and if He were non-existent, He would resemble non-existents; so He is neither existent nor non-existent. As for the modern Batiniyyah, represented in the nihilism of Sergei Nechaev, the atheism of Marx, the destructiveness of Freud, and the descendants of world Judaism represented by Darwin, they say: religion is a myth. And Almighty God speaks the truth when He says: 'Great is the word that comes out of their mouths; they say nothing but a lie' (1). And in His saying: 'They are but like cattle; nay, they are even more astray' (2). These are the intellectual weapons prepared by the school of the Qur'an. And now we have the right to ask: what does it [kalam] have to enter with them into the battles of new blatant atheism? The brazen atheism debates our youth with a weapon they do not know, and a method they have not known. Our sons know nothing about the indivisible part, nor about the individual substance, nor about nonexistence and existence according to Sartre and his existentialism, and they have not trained in the deception of modern standards. The standards of Satan, which destroy and do not build, corrupt and do not reform, cause doubt and do not bring certainty. Then what is the opinion of the scholars of 'Aqeedah? Commentary
This passage is a polemical introduction that argues for the necessity of the science of kalam (dialectical theology) in confronting modern heresies. The author begins by posing a rhetorical question: can we learn from the origin of kalam and its continuance? He then states that the negative answer (that Muslims no longer need kalam) is false, because we need it to refute the tyranny of the twentieth-century Jahmiyyah (anthropomorphists who also denied the uncreatedness of the Qur'an). He contrasts the old Jahmiyyah, who said the Qur'an is created, with the modern Jahmiyyah (Orientalism and missionary followers) who assert the human authorship of the Qur'an — that Muhammad composed it as a reflection of Arabian society. Next, he asks how to debate destructive modern schools: the Nominalists (who deny that the word 'Allah' refers to a reality), the Empiricists (exemplified by Feuerbach's progression from God to reason to man), and the Rationalists (who call this the age of enlightenment and seek to replace religion with reason, as per Wolff). He then compares the ancient Batiniyyah (esotericists) who denied God's existence by saying He is neither existent nor non-existent, to the modern Batiniyyah (nihilism of Nechaev, atheism of Marx, destructiveness of Freud, and Darwinian evolution) who declare religion a myth and prophets liars. He cites verses from the Qur'an condemning their words. He then asks what intellectual weapons the 'school of the Qur'an' has prepared to fight these new waves of atheism, noting that the arrogant atheism uses arguments youth are unfamiliar with — such as the indivisible atom, the individual substance, and Sartre's existential concepts — and that they have not been trained in these deceptive modern standards. He ends with a call to scholars of 'Aqeedah to respond.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that classical Islamic theology (kalam) is irrelevant to modern challenges.
Correction: This passage shows that modern heresies are direct descendants of ancient ones, and kalam provides the necessary tools to refute them.

A) They say the Qur'an is created.
B) They say the Qur'an is the eternal speech of God.
C) They say the Qur'an is human-authored.
D) They say the Qur'an is a myth.

Key Takeaway
The science of kalam remains indispensable for responding to contemporary heresies, which are often modern expressions of ancient deviations.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you encounter someone who claims the Quran is of human authorship, do you have a reasoned response ready, or do you feel inadequate? Will you commit to learning kalam to be prepared?
?
Have you ever allowed doubts from modern philosophies to creep into your heart without actively seeking their refutation? Will you now seek to understand the refutations that the scholars of kalam have provided?
?
If the author asked you today, 'What is your weapon against the atheism that attacks our youth?', could you answer with confidence, or would you remain silent? What step will you take to change that?

Definition and Linguistic Origins of Jadal
Author contrasts jadal with kalam and gives lexical definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the science of jadal is defined in contrast to kalam and what its linguistic roots mean according to classical Arabic lexicons.

Core Idea
Jadal is the polemical weapon of opponents, rooted in linguistic meanings of twisting and overpowering.

In our study of kalam, we saw a rational defense of faith. But what about the arguments used by those who oppose Ahl al-Sunnah? This passage introduces the science of jadal, the dialectical tool of heretical sects, and traces its name back to its original Arabic meanings. By exploring these lexicons, we uncover how language shapes the concept of disputation.

Guided Path
1
Distinguish jadal from kalam
Read the opening sentences where the author contrasts kalam (defensive rational science) with the 'other science' used by opponents. Notice the rhetorical question.
This sets the stage for why jadal matters.
2
Identify the opponents
The author names specific groups (Khawarij, Batiniyyah, Jahmiyyah) that use jadal. Reflect on why these groups are singled out.
3
Examine the first linguistic definition
Read Ibn Sayyidah's definition: 'jadala' means to twist firmly, and 'jadal' means throwing to the ground. Consider how this physical imagery applies to argumentation.
The ground (jadalah) symbolizes hardness and finality.
4
Examine the second linguistic definition
Read the entry from Lisan al-'Arab: 'jadal' is stubbornness and skill in argument, with derivatives like 'jadalahu' (to debate) and 'jadal' (strong debater). Note the emphasis on overcoming.
Both definitions converge on the idea of overpowering an opponent.
5
Summarize the lexical core
Combine the two definitions: jadal linguistically implies forceful, stubborn, and victorious disputation. This lays the foundation for understanding the science itself.

The science of jadal: its origin and development
We said at the beginning of our discussion about the science of kalam: if this is a science that defends by it the creedal beliefs and its defensive weapons are the intellect and its tools, then what is the other science called...?
The science that the opponents of Ahl al-Sunnah wa al-Jama'ah—from the Khawarij, the Batiniyyah, the Jahmiyyah, and the rest of the misguided sects that deviate from the madhhab of the Salaf—use.
And when
Yes. What is this science called? Indeed we see, and many scholars and thinkers share our opinion, that it is the science of jadal. If that is the case, then what is its origin, and what are the stages it passed through until it reached us...? Ibn Sayyidah says: 'jadala al-shay'a yajduluhu jadlan: he twisted it firmly. And al-jadal means throwing down on al-jadalah (the ground), so called because of its hardness (1).'
And the author of Lisan al-'Arab says: 'al-jadal linguistically is stubbornness in argumentation, and the ability for it. And one says jadalahu mujadalah and jidalan, and a man is jadal and mijdal: strong in argumentation. It is said: jadaltu al-rajul fajadaltuhu jadlan, meaning I overcame him. And a man is jadal if he is stronger in disputation. And the noun is al-jadal (2), which is vehemence in argumentation.'
(1) Asas al-Balaghah by al-Zamakhshari p. 111, and Lisan al-'Arab p. 103 vol. 11.
•
(2) Cf. Lisan al-'Arab vol. 11 p. 105.
47
Source pages p. 46

Commentary
This passage defines the science of jadal (dialectic) and distinguishes it from kalam. The author begins by recalling that kalam defends Islamic creedal beliefs using rational arguments and tools. He then asks: what is the other science used by opponents of Ahl al-Sunnah—such as the Khawarij, Batiniyya, Jahmiyya, and other deviant sects? The answer is that this science is jadal. Having identified it, he raises the question of its origin and development. To ground the discussion, he provides linguistic definitions from classical lexicons. First, he cites Ibn Sayyidah, who explains that 'jadala' originally means to twist firmly, and that 'jadal' means throwing someone onto the ground (al-jadalah), metaphorically indicating overpowering in argument. Second, he quotes the author of Lisan al-'Arab, who defines jadal linguistically as stubbornness and skill in argumentation. He gives morphological derivatives: the verb jadalahu (he debated him), the noun jidalan/jadalan, and the adjectives jadal and mijdal (strong debater). The phrase 'jadaltuhu' means 'I overcame him.' The passage concludes that the noun 'al-jadal' signifies vehemence in argumentation. The footnotes reference standard Arabic lexicons.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think jadal and kalam are the same or interchangeable sciences.
Correction: The passage clarifies that jadal is specifically the polemical method used by opponents of Ahl al-Sunnah, whereas kalam is the rational defense of orthodox creed.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the linguistic meaning of 'jadal' as defined by Ibn Sayyidah?
A) Skill in reasoning
B) Firm twisting and throwing down
C) Love of argument
D) Knowledge of the intellect

Key Takeaway
Jadal linguistically means forceful, stubborn argument aimed at overpowering an opponent, reflecting its use as the dialectical weapon of heretical sects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever found yourself in a religious debate where your real goal was to 'overpower' the other person rather than to reach the truth? How does the linguistic meaning of jadal as 'throwing to the ground' expose the spiritual danger in such an attitude? What will you change in your next discussion?
?
The passage says jadal is the tool of opponents of Ahl al-Sunnah. In your own conversations, are you using jadal-style argumentation without realizing it—seeking to win rather than to enlighten? How can you consciously shift from jadal to kalam—defending truth with humility and intellect? Write down one concrete habit you will adopt.
?
When you hear the Arabic root 'j-d-l' which includes 'twisting', consider your own speech: Do you ever subtly twist facts or words to win an argument? How does knowing this root call you to examine your intentions in discussion? Make a commitment today to speak with integrity, even if it means losing a debate.

The Nature of Jadl: Method or Art?
Defining jadl through philosophical and lexical analysis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the differing views of Aristotle and Ibn Sina on whether jadl is a method of reasoning or a distinct logical art, and the implications of this difference.

Core Idea
Jadl is either a universal method or a specialized art—this shapes its logical status.

Before we explore the Quranic uses of jadl, we need to clarify what jadl means in the logical tradition. The great philosophers Aristotle and Ibn Sina disagreed on whether jadl is a general method or a distinct art. This passage presents their views and al-Firuzabadi's lexical definition, setting the stage for a deeper understanding.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three elements of jadl
The passage begins by listing three components of jadl: the way, inference from common notions, and absence of contradiction. Note how Aristotle and Ibn Sina agree on two but differ on the first.
The first element is the key point of divergence.
2
Contrast Aristotle and Ibn Sina
Aristotle sees jadl as a method; Ibn Sina sees it as an art. Reflect on the implications: for Aristotle, jadl is part of general logic; for Ibn Sina, it is a specialized type of syllogism.
3
Understand al-Firuzabadi's definition
Read the lexical definition: jadl as opposition in contention and overcoming, from twisting. This adds a practical, adversarial dimension.
The etymology hints at the combative nature of jadl.

( أ ) الطريق . (ب) الاستدلال من المشهورات. (جـ) عدم التناقض عند الجواب . ويتفق أرسطو وابن سينا في العنصرين الأخيرين، ويختلفان في العنصر الأول. فالجدل عند أرسطو : طريق أو منهج ، وعند ابن سينا صناعة(۱) هذا الفرق له أثره فى اعتبار الجدل ملحقاً بالمنطق العام لأرسطو، أو هو نوع من القياس، ولكنه ليس يقيناً، أي ان المنطق واحد، وطريقه واحد، أعلاه البرهان. والجدل مرتبة أضعف منه، وأما في اعتبار الجدل نوعاً متميزاً من المنطق وصناعة خاصة هي الصناعة الجدلية . وإذا أردنا أن نتعرف على رأي مجد الدين محمد بن يعقوب الفيروز بادي . في تعريف الجدل فإنه يقول : الجدل: هو المعارضة على سبيل المنازعة والمغالبة، وأصله من جدل الشيء أحكم فتله ، كأن كلا من المتجادلين يقتل الآخر عن رأيه . وقد ورد الجدل في القرآن على وجوه مختلفة . الأول: معارضة نوح وقومه . قال تعالى : يا نُوحُ قَدْ جَادَلْتَنَا فَأَكْثَرْتَ جَدَالَنَا . الثاني : مجادلة أهل العدوان . قال تعالى : (أَتُجَادِلُونَني في أَسْمَاء سميتموها) . الثالث : جدال إبراهيم والملائكة في باب قوم لوط . قال تعالى : (يُجَادِلُنَا في قَوْمِ لُوطٍ ) . الرابع : جدال صناديد قريش في إثبات إله العالمين. قال تعالى : يُجَادِلُونَ في اللَّهِ . وجدال الكفار في القرآن. قال تعالى : إنَّ الَّذِينَ يُجَادِلُونَ فِي آيَاتِ ... وجدال المنكرين في إنكار الحجة والبرهان بالشبهة والبطلان . قال تعالى : وجَادَلُوا بِالْبَاطِل ليُدْخِضُوا بِهِ الحَقُّ . وجدال النبي صلى الله عليه وسلم في باب الخائنين والمنافقين . قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا تُجَادِلْ عَنِ الَّذِينَ يَخْتَانُونَ أَنْفُسَهُمْ ) وجدال الصحابة في حقهم . قال تعالى : مَا أَنتُمْ هَؤُلاء جَادَلْتُمْ عَنْهُمْ فِي الحَيَاةِ الدُّنْيَا فَمَنْ يُجَادِلُ الله عَنْهُمْ . وجدال النبي أهل الكتاب باللطف والإحسان . قال تعالى: ﴿ وَجَادِهُمْ بالتي هي أَحْسَنُ) . وجدال الصحابة إياهم . قال تعالى : ولا تُجَادِلُوا أَهْلَ الكِتَابِ إلا بالتي هي أحْسَنُ) . وجدال بمعنى الخصومة بين الحجاج. قال تعالى: ﴿وَلَا جِدَالَ في الحج).
(a) The way. (b) Inference from common notions. (c) Absence of contradiction in the answer. Aristotle and Ibn Sina agree on the last two elements, and differ on the first. For Aristotle, jadl is a way or method, and for Ibn Sina it is an art. This difference has its effect in considering jadl as attached to general logic for Aristotle, or it is a type of syllogism, but not certain, meaning that logic is one and its way is one, the highest of which is demonstration. Jadl is a weaker rank than it, and as for considering jadl as a distinct type of logic and a special art, it is the dialectical art. And if we want to know the opinion of Majd al-Din Muhammad ibn Ya'qub al-Firuzabadi concerning the definition of jadl, he says: Jadl is opposition in the manner of contention and overcoming, and its origin is from jadala al-shay' (to twist something firmly), as if each of the two disputants twists the other from his opinion. And jadl has occurred in the Quran in various aspects. The first: the opposition of Noah and his people. He said, exalted: "O Noah, you have disputed with us and multiplied our disputation." The second: the disputation of the people of aggression. He said, exalted: "Do you dispute with me concerning names you have named?" The third: the disputation of Abraham and the angels concerning the people of Lot. He said, exalted: "He disputes with Us concerning the people of Lot." The fourth: the disputation of the leaders of Quraysh concerning establishing the God of the worlds. He said, exalted: "They dispute concerning God." And the disputation of the disbelievers in the Quran. He said, exalted: "Indeed, those who dispute concerning Our signs..." And the disputation of the deniers in denying proof and evidence with doubt and falsehood. He said, exalted: "And they disputed with falsehood to refute thereby the truth." And the disputation of the Prophet, peace be upon him, concerning the betrayers and hypocrites. He said, exalted: "And do not argue on behalf of those who betray themselves." And the disputation of the Companions concerning their right. He said, exalted: "Here you are — you argued on their behalf in the worldly life; then who will argue with God on their behalf?" And the disputation of the Prophet with the People of the Book with gentleness and kindness. He said, exalted: "And dispute with them in a way that is best." And the disputation of the Companions with them. He said, exalted: "And do not argue with the People of the Book except in a way that is best." And jadl meaning quarrel among pilgrims. He said, exalted: "And no disputation during Hajj."
Source pages p. 49

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and illustrative survey of the term 'jadl' (dialectic/disputation). It begins by contrasting Aristotle and Ibn Sina on the nature of jadl. For Aristotle, jadl is a method or path of reasoning; for Ibn Sina, it is a distinct logical art. This difference affects whether jadl is seen as subordinate to general logic (Aristotle) or as a specialized form of syllogism, weaker than demonstration. The passage then introduces al-Firuzabadi's lexical definition: jadl is opposition in a contentious and dominant manner, etymologically from twisting something tightly, implying each disputant twists the other from his position. Finally, the author lists various Quranic occurrences of jadl, each illustrating a different context: Noah's people disputing against him, the disputation of the unjust, Abraham's argument with angels, Quraysh's denial of God, disbelievers rejecting signs, deniers using falsehood, the prohibition against arguing for betrayers, the Companions' argument in this world, the command to argue gently with People of the Book, and the prohibition of argument during Hajj. This enumeration shows that jadl in the Quran is not monolithic but contextually diverse, ranging from blameworthy contention to permitted or even commanded polite debate.

Key Terms
الجدل
المعارضة
المنازعة
المغالبة
صناعة جدلية
البرهان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Jadl is simply a synonym for 'argument' with no technical nuance.
Correction: Jadl has a specific logical meaning involving common premises and non-contradiction, and it is distinguished from demonstration.

Checkpoint
On which element do Aristotle and Ibn Sina disagree?
A) Inference from common notions
B) Absence of contradiction
C) The way (method vs art)
D) The definition of al-Firuzabadi

Key Takeaway
Jadl is defined both logically (as a method or art) and lexically (as contentious opposition), and its status varies between philosophers.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you engage in discussions about your faith, do you find yourself trying to 'twist' the other person's words like a rope, as the lexical meaning of jadl suggests? How can you ensure your debates seek truth rather than victory?
?
The passage ranks jadl as weaker than burhan (certain demonstration). Think of a belief you hold strongly: have you ever settled for weak arguments (jadl) when you could have pursued certain proof? What step will you take today to move toward burhan in that area?
?
If jadl is defined as opposition and contention, then how many of your arguments with loved ones have been true jadl—twisting their opinions—rather than sincere dialogue? Will you commit to checking your intent before your next religious discussion?

Quranic Varieties of Jadl
Enumerating Quranic instances of jadl with brief contextual notes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the diverse contexts in which the Quran uses the term 'jadl' (disputation), ranging from blameworthy contention to praiseworthy debate.

Core Idea
Jadl in the Quran is not monolithic; it includes both condemned and commanded forms.

Having defined jadl philosophically, the author turns to the Quran to show its actual usage. The Quran mentions jadl in many situations: from the stubborn disputation of Noah's people to the gentle debate commanded with the People of the Book. This passage lists nine distinct Quranic occurrences, each illustrating a different shade of meaning.

Guided Path
1
Scan the list of Quranic examples
Read through all the examples from the first (Noah) to the last (Hajj). Notice the variety of subjects: prophets, disbelievers, angels, Companions.
Some are blameworthy, some are neutral, some are praiseworthy.
2
Categorize by tone
Group the examples into those where jadl is condemned (e.g., disbelievers arguing falsely) and those where it is permitted or even commanded (e.g., arguing gently with People of the Book).
The verse about Hajj prohibits jadl altogether.
3
Reflect on the purpose
Ask why the Quran uses the same word for such different contexts. The answer lies in the nature of the disputation—its method and intention.
This shows that jadl is a neutral tool; its moral value depends on how and why it is used.

Misconception: Jadl is always forbidden in Islam because the Quran condemns arguing.

Which of the following Quranic contexts involves a command to dispute in the best manner?
A) The disputation of Noah's people
B) The disputation of Abraham with the angels
C) The disputation with the People of the Book
D) The prohibition of disputation during Hajj

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
This lesson shows that even the Prophet was told not to argue on behalf of betrayers. Is there someone in your life whose wrongdoing you have been excusing or defending through argument? Are you willing to stop that jadl today?
?
The Quran prohibits all disputation during Hajj, a sacred act. What does this reveal about the priority of worship over winning arguments? In your own moments of worship—prayer, fasting, charity—do you abandon contentious debate, or do you bring it with you into the masjid? What will you change?

The Origin of the Science of Jadal (Disputation)
The author discusses the origin of jadal as a universal human and even angelic phenomenon.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the science of jadal (disputation) is not a later invention but a universal phenomenon rooted in human nature and even present among angels.

Core Idea
Jadal is as old as humanity — and even angels disputed with Allah.

Before delving into the rules and methods of jadal, we must first appreciate its origin. This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid situates disputation as an innate human drive, citing Quranic examples from the angels and the Day of Judgment. Understanding this root helps us see why jadal is both natural and divinely sanctioned within limits.

Guided Path
1
Read the Passage and Translation
Read the Arabic passage once for its overall flow, then read the English translation to ensure comprehension.
Pay attention to the Quranic verses quoted.
2
Identify the Main Claim
Identify the author's central claim about the origin of jadal. Notice the phrases that indicate universality.
Look for keywords like 'جبلة' (innate disposition).
3
Examine the Quranic Evidence
The passage gives two Quranic proofs: the angels' objection to Adam's creation and the disputation on the Day of Judgment. Note how each supports the claim.
The angels' example shows jadal even before humans existed.
4
Reflect on Significance
Consider why establishing jadal as innate matters for the study of kalam. How does this view affect the legitimacy of theological debate?
This connects to the broader methodology of Islamic theology.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نشأة علم الجدل :
يرى كثير من المفكرين والعلماء أن الجدل ظاهرة إنسانية وجدت مع وجود الإنسان على ظهر البسيطة .
بل إن هذه الظاهرة وجدت في غير الجنس البشري كالملائكة وإبليس ويعللون ذلك أن النفس البشرية مجبولة على حب الدفاع عنها، وتقريب مطالبها الدنيوية، ولن يتم لها ذلك إلا باستعمالها أسلحة الجدل وقوارع الحجج، وهذه الظاهرة لن يتخلى عنها البشر حتى في ساحة الحشر ويوم الحساب الأعظم، قال تعالى : يَوْمَ تَأْتِي كُلُّ نَفْسٍ تُجَادِلُ عَنْ نَفْسِهَا.
والقرآن الكريم يحدثنا عن موقف الملائكة من ربهم عندما أخبرهم بقوله: إِنِّي جَاعِلٌ فِي الْأَرْضِ خَلِيفَةً قَالُوا : أَتَجْعَلُ فِيهَا مَنْ يُفْسِدُ فِيهَا وَيَسْفِكُ الدِّمَاءَ وَنَحْنُ نُسَبِّحُ بِحَمْدِكَ وَنُقَدِّسُ لَكَ قَالَ إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ.
The origin of the science of jadal: Many thinkers and scholars hold that jadal is a human phenomenon that existed with the existence of man on the face of the earth. Rather, this phenomenon existed in other than the human species, such as the angels and Iblis. They explain that the human soul is instinctively inclined to love defending itself and fulfilling its worldly needs, and it cannot achieve that except by using the weapons of jadal and decisive proofs. And humans will not abandon this phenomenon even in the arena of the Gathering and the Great Day of Reckoning. He said, the Exalted: 'The Day every soul will come disputing on its own behalf.' And the Noble Quran tells us about the position of the angels with their Lord when He informed them with His statement: 'Indeed, I will make upon the earth a vicegerent.' They said: 'Will You place upon it one who causes corruption therein and sheds blood, while we exalt You with praise and sanctify You?' He said: 'Indeed, I know that which you do not know.'
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage addresses the origin (nash'a) of the science of jadal (disputation). The author states a common view among thinkers and scholars: jadal is a human phenomenon that has existed since the appearance of humans on earth. Moreover, it is found even in non-human beings like the angels and Iblis. The reason, it is said, is that the human soul is instinctively inclined to defend itself and pursue its worldly goals, and this can only be achieved by using the weapons of jadal and compelling arguments. This tendency remains with humans even on the Day of Resurrection, as the Quran says that on that day every soul will come disputing for itself (Surah al-Nahl 16:111). Then the author cites the famous Quranic account of the angels' objection to God's plan to create Adam: they argued that humans would cause corruption and shed blood, while they themselves glorify God. This shows that even angels engaged in a form of jadal with their Lord. Thus, the phenomenon of disputation is deeply rooted in creation and is not limited to human beings.

Key Terms
جدل (jadal)
نشأة (nash'a)
جبلة (jibilla)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think jadal is a developed science only in Islamic civilization, a later human invention.
Correction: The author roots jadal in human nature and even in the angelic realm, showing it is innate and universal.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which non-human beings are mentioned as engaging in jadal?
A) Jinn and animals
B) Angels and Iblis
C) Prophets and saints
D) Only humans

Key Takeaway
Jadal is not a later human invention but an innate disposition shared by humans and even angels, as shown in the Quran.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you catch yourself arguing passionately for your own opinion, do you see it as a natural instinct (jibilla) or as something you should suppress? How does knowing that even angels engaged in jadal with Allah change your view of your own debating tendencies?
?
The angels questioned Allah's wisdom but then submitted to His knowledge. Is there an area in your life where you resist accepting a truth because you think you know better? What would it look like for you to say, 'إِنِّي أَعْلَمُ مَا لَا تَعْلَمُونَ' to yourself?
?
On the Day of Judgment, every soul will be disputing on its own behalf. What does that reveal about the depth of self-defense in your heart? Are you prepared to stand before Allah still defending yourself, or are you willing to surrender your arguments now?

Jadal as an Innate Human Trait
Defines jadal and cites Quranic evidence for its innateness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that jadal (disputation) is an innate human quality, grounded in Quranic evidence and linguistic roots, and that the science of jadal emerged as a result of this innate disposition.

Core Idea
Jadal is not a learned skill—it is built into human nature.

This lesson introduces the concept of jadal, often translated as disputation or argumentation. The author begins by anchoring jadal in the Quran and showing that it is a fundamental trait of human beings, present from the beginning and even shared by angels and Iblis. Understanding jadal as an innate faculty sets the stage for studying its proper use and rules.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Get a first impression of the text's claims about jadal. Notice the Quranic verses and the etymological explanation.
The passage is dense with evidence; read slowly.
2
Identify the Quranic evidence
Find the three verses cited: 43:58, 18:54, and 16:111. Note what each verse demonstrates about jadal.
The first verse shows jadal in a historical context, the second declares it innate, the third shows its persistence.
3
Reflect on the etymology and universality
Consider the linguistic origin of jadal as wrestling on hard ground. Then ponder the claim that jadal exists even among angels and Iblis. Why does this matter for the study of jadal?
This reinforces that jadal is a universal, not merely theological, phenomenon.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وجدال ابن الزبعرى في حق عيسى وعزير والأصنام . قال تعالى: ﴿مَا ضَرَبُوهُ لَك إِلَّا جَدَلاً ) (٢) . وجدال موجود في جبلة الإنسان. قال تعالى: ﴿وَكَانَ الإِنْسَانُ أَكْثَرَ شَيءٍ جَدَلاً ) (۳) . وقيل الأصل في الجدل: الصراع وإسقاط الإنسان صاحبه على الجدالة أي الأرض الصلبة . نشأة علم الجدل : يرى كثير من المفكرين والعلماء أن الجدل ظاهرة إنسانية وجدت مع وجود الإنسان على ظهر البسيطة . بل إن هذه الظاهرة وجدت في غير الجنس البشري كالملائكة وإبليس ويعللون ذلك أن النفس البشرية مجبولة على حب الدفاع عنها، وتقريب مطالبها الدنيوية، ولن يتم لها ذلك إلا باستعمالها أسلحة الجدل وقوارع الحجج، وهذه الظاهرة لن يتخلى عنها البشر حتى في ساحة الحشر ويوم الحساب الأعظم، قال تعالى : يَوْمَ تَأتي كُلُّ نَفْسٍ تُجَادِلُ عَنْ نَفْسِهَا ) (٤) .
And there was disputation (jadal) of Ibn al-Ziba'ra regarding Jesus, Ezra, and the idols. Allah said: 'They did not strike it for you except as disputation' (43:58). And disputation exists in the innate disposition (jibla) of man. Allah said: 'And man is most disputatious of things' (18:54). And it was said: the origin of jadal is wrestling and throwing a person onto the hard ground (jadala). The emergence of the science of jadal: Many thinkers and scholars see that jadal is a human phenomenon that existed with the existence of man on the face of the earth. Rather, this phenomenon existed in other than the human species, such as the angels and Iblis. And they explain that the human soul is innately disposed to love defending itself and attaining its worldly demands, and it cannot achieve that except by using the weapons of jadal and decisive proofs. And this phenomenon will not be abandoned by humans even in the arena of the Gathering and the Great Day of Reckoning. Allah said: 'The day every soul will come disputing for itself' (16:111).
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage defines jadal and demonstrates its innate character. The author begins by citing two Quranic instances of jadal: the pagans' disputation with the Prophet concerning Jesus and Ezra (Q. 43:58), and the general statement that man is by nature argumentative (Q. 18:54). Etymologically, jadal is derived from the word for wrestling on hard ground, implying a struggle. The author then turns to the origin of the science of jadal, stating that it is a universal human phenomenon found even among angels and Iblis. The explanation given is that the human soul is naturally driven to defend itself and secure worldly advantages, requiring argumentation. This trait persists even on the Day of Judgment, as confirmed by Quran 16:111. The passage establishes that jadal is not an acquired discipline but an ingrained characteristic of both humans and spiritual beings.

Key Terms
جدل (jadal)
جبلة (jibla)
نشأة علم الجدل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some may think jadal is a purely Islamic theological art.
Correction: The passage shows jadal is a universal human trait, present from the beginning and even in non-human beings.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the linguistic origin of the word 'jadal'?
A) From the Greek word for dialectic
B) From wrestling on hard ground
C) From the Quranic term for debate
D) From the name of a famous disputant

Key Takeaway
Jadal is an innate human trait, confirmed by Quran, etymology, and its universal presence even among spiritual beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you find yourself in a heated argument, do you recognize it as your innate tendency to defend yourself, or do you assume you are simply right? How will you remind yourself that jadal is a natural human trait that must be purified for the sake of truth?
?
The passage says jadal continues even on the Day of Judgment, when every soul will come disputing for itself. What kind of defense will you offer on that day? Are you building your arguments now on solid evidence or on pride?
?
Knowing that jadal originated from wrestling—a struggle to throw someone down—do you see your arguments as a means to dominate others or to reach the truth? What concrete step can you take today to ensure your disputes are sincere and not merely self-defence?
