Al-Jahiz's Praise of the Book
Al-Jahiz enumerates the benefits of the written book.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jahiz's argument for the indispensability of the written book for preserving knowledge, enabling governance, and sustaining religion and worldly life.

Core Idea
The pen transcends time; the book is the pillar of civilization.

Al-Jahiz, the renowned prose stylist, offers a spirited defense of the written word. In this passage, he explains why the book is not merely a luxury but a necessity for human society—from preserving the past to governing distant lands. Let's explore his vivid contrast between the tongue and the pen.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let al-Jahiz's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the rhetorical flow: from loss to contrast to expansion to climax.
2
Identify the key contrast
Al-Jahiz sets the tongue against the pen. The tongue serves only the present, while the pen reaches the absent, past, and future. This contrast is central to his argument.
3
Trace the argument's expansion
Observe how al-Jahiz moves from preservation of knowledge to administrative needs, then to governance, and finally to the foundation of religion and worldly life.
Each step broadens the scope of the book's utility.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Jahiz ends by stating that the pillar of religion and worldly life stands through the book and calculation. Consider what he means by 'calculation' and why it is paired with the book.
This concluding statement underscores the book's comprehensive importance.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصور الجاحظ مهمة الكتاب وأثره في دنيا الناس ماضيهم وحاضرهم ومدح فيقول : ولولا الكتاب لاختلت أخبار الماضين وانقطعت آثار الغائبين، وإنما اللسان للشاهد لك والقلم للغائب عنك ، والماضي قبلك، والغابر بعدك فصار نفعه أعم، والدواوين إليه أفقر، والملك المقيم بالواسطة لا يدرك مصالح أطرافه، وسد ثغوره وتقويم سكان مملكته إلا بالكتاب، ولولا الكتاب لما تم تدبير ولا استقامت الأمور، وقد رأينا عمود صلاح الدين والدنيا إنما يعتدل في نصابه، ويقوم على أساسه بالكتاب والحساب» (۲).
And al-Jāḥiẓ depicts the mission of the book and its effect on the world of people, their past and present, and praises, saying: Were it not for the book, the reports of the past would be defective and the traces of the absent would be cut off. Indeed, the tongue is for the present witness, and the pen is for the one absent from you, the past before you, and the future after you. So its benefit became more general, and the administrative offices (dawāwīn) are most in need of it. And the king residing in the center cannot perceive the interests of his regions, nor close his gaps, nor straighten the inhabitants of his kingdom except through the book. And were it not for the book, no administration would be completed nor would affairs be upright. And we have seen that the pillar of religion and worldly life is only balanced in its position and stands on its foundation through the book and calculation.» (2)
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents a quotation from al-Jāḥiẓ praising the book. It is a piece of descriptive exposition that extols the book's indispensable role in preserving knowledge of the past and communicating with the absent. Al-Jāḥiẓ contrasts the tongue, which is limited to the immediate presence, with the pen, which transcends time and space: the tongue serves the witness present before you, while the pen serves the absent, the past, and the future. Consequently, the book's benefit is universal, and administrative departments (dawāwīn) are most in need of it. Al-Jāḥiẓ then extends the argument to governance: a ruler residing in the center cannot manage the affairs of distant regions, secure borders, or set right the population without the book. He concludes that the very pillar of religion and worldly life is established only through the book and calculation. The passage thus functions as a rhetorical celebration of the written word as the foundation of culture and administration.

Key Terms
كتاب
لسان
قلم
تدبير
ديوان

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Many think al-Jahiz's praise of the book is only about scholarly or literary uses, ignoring its role in governance and administration.
Correction: Al-Jahiz explicitly includes the king's need for the book to manage borders and subjects, and concludes that the book is the pillar of religion and worldly life.

Checkpoint
According to al-Jāḥiẓ, what is the benefit of the pen compared to the tongue?
A) The tongue is more reliable than the pen.
B) The pen serves the absent, the past, and the future.
C) The pen is only useful for poetry.
D) The tongue is for writing letters.

Key Takeaway
Al-Jahiz argues that the book is essential not only for preserving knowledge but also for effective governance and as the foundation of both religion and worldly life.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on books (or digital texts) daily. When was the last time you truly appreciated the written word as a pillar of your faith and life? What would be lost if all written knowledge vanished?
?
Al-Jahiz says the pen reaches the absent and the future. Your own writings—emails, notes, social media posts—will outlive you. Are you writing what you want to be remembered for? Or are you leaving a legacy of triviality?
?
The book is essential for governance and administration. In your own responsibilities (work, family, community), do you treat written records as foundational, or do you rely on memory and oral communication? What one change could you make to strengthen your use of writing for order and clarity?

Al-Jahiz on the Indispensability of the Book
Al-Jahiz praises the book's role in preserving knowledge and governance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand al-Jahiz's argument for the indispensable role of the written book in preserving knowledge and enabling effective governance.

Core Idea
The book transcends time and distance, making it essential for knowledge and rule.

Al-Jahiz, the celebrated 9th-century scholar, presents a compelling case for the book's unique role. He argues that while speech serves the present, the written word bridges past, future, and distance. This passage introduces his reasoning and its implications for both scholarship and statecraft.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the English translation. Let al-Jahiz's own words establish the argument before you analyze it.
Notice the strong opening: 'Were it not for the book...'
2
Examine the key contrast
Al-Jahiz contrasts the tongue (لسان) with the pen (قلم). Identify what each represents and why the pen is more far-reaching.
The tongue is for the present witness; the pen reaches the absent, the past, and the future.
3
Apply to governance
Consider how al-Jahiz extends the book's importance to the ruler. Why does a king need the book to manage his kingdom?
The king governs by proxy and cannot be everywhere; the book transmits his commands.
4
Reflect on the final pillar
Al-Jahiz concludes that the book and calculation (الحساب) are the foundation of religious and worldly reform. Think about why he pairs 'book' with 'calculation'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ويصور الجاحظ مهمة الكتاب وأثره في دنيا الناس ماضيهم وحاضرهم ومدح فيقول : ولولا الكتاب لاختلت أخبار الماضين وانقطعت آثار الغائبين، وإنما اللسان للشاهد لك والقلم للغائب عنك ، والماضي قبلك، والغابر بعدك فصار نفعه أعم، والدواوين إليه أفقر، والملك المقيم بالواسطة لا يدرك مصالح أطرافه، وسد ثغوره وتقويم سكان مملكته إلا بالكتاب، ولولا الكتاب لما تم تدبير ولا استقامت الأمور، وقد رأينا عمود صلاح الدين والدنيا إنما يعتدل في نصابه، ويقوم على أساسه بالكتاب والحساب
And al-Jahiz depicts the mission of the book and its impact on people's lives, past and present, and praises, saying: Were it not for the book, the reports of the past would have been corrupted and the traces of the absent would have been cut off. Indeed, the tongue is for the present witness, and the pen for the one absent from you, and the past before you, and the one to come after you. So its benefit became more general, and the registries are more in need of it. The king who governs by proxy cannot grasp the interests of his borders, fill his gaps, and rectify the inhabitants of his kingdom except through the book. And were it not for the book, no administration would be completed nor would affairs be upright. And we have seen that the pillar of the rectification of religion and this world only stands straight in its position and is established on its foundation through the book and calculation.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage presents al-Jahiz's argument for the indispensability of the written word. It begins by stating that he praises the book's role. Then he explains that without books, historical knowledge would be lost and the deeds of the absent would be unknown. He contrasts the spoken word, which is limited to the present, with the pen, which reaches the absent, the past, and the future. The book's benefit is thus universal. He then applies this to governance: a king who rules indirectly needs the book to manage distant lands, defend frontiers, and administer justice. Finally, he asserts that the foundation of religious and worldly reform is built on the book and calculation.

Key Terms
الكتاب
اللسان
القلم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think al-Jahiz is dismissing oral transmission entirely.
Correction: Al-Jahiz is not diminishing oral transmission but emphasizing the complementarity of writing; he acknowledges the tongue's role for the present while elevating the pen for what is absent.

Checkpoint

A) Only historical knowledge would be lost.
B) Only governance would fail.
C) Both knowledge and governance would be impaired.
D) Nothing would change.

Key Takeaway
The written book is indispensable for preserving knowledge across time and for governing distant lands, forming the foundation of both religious and worldly order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You rely on memory for most of your Islamic knowledge—how has that led to forgetfulness or distortion? What specific area of your deen will you now commit to writing down after reading al-Jahiz's argument?
?
Think of a responsibility you have (parenting, work, community). Without a written plan or record, how have you failed to 'grasp the interests of your borders'? What is one book or document you will create this week to strengthen that area?
?
Al-Jahiz says the pillar of religion and the world stands on the book and calculation. In your life, which of these two is weaker? What is one action you will take today to strengthen that pillar?

The Mosque as the First Islamic University
Describes the mosque's role in education and the first mosque (Quba).

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the mosque functioned as the primary institution for Islamic education and community life, with the Mosque of Quba as its first model.

Core Idea
The mosque was history's first comprehensive university.

This passage from the coursebook highlights the central role of the mosque in early Islamic education. While we often think of mosques as places of prayer, they were the original campuses where all knowledge was taught. The author begins by asserting the book's importance, then immediately elevates the mosque as the leader in education and life. He grounds this in the Quranic verse about Masjid Quba, establishing it as the prototype.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's claim about the mosque's educational leadership come through clearly.
Notice the author's flow: from book to mosque to the specific example of Quba.
2
Reflect on the Quranic verse
The author cites Surah Al-Tawbah verse 108 about Masjid Quba. Ask yourself why this verse is used to support the mosque's role in education. What does 'founded on piety' imply?
3
Trace the continuity of study circles
The passage states that study circles (halaqat) arose in mosques and continued uninterrupted. Think about how this practice made mosques de facto universities.
This connects to the common misunderstanding that mosques are only for prayer.

In it are men who love to purify themselves, and Allah loves those who purify themselves.' And talk about the mosque is in reality talk about the main university for spreading all kinds of Islamic knowledge and culture in its various ways and diverse branches. And the observer of mosques in Islamic cities sees that study circles arose in the mosque and continued in that manner over the years and centuries, and in different regions, without interruption.

This passage establishes the mosque as the original Islamic university. It begins by acknowledging the book's role, then asserts that the mosque led all aspects of education and life. It cites the Quranic verse praising the Mosque of Quba as founded on piety, implying its suitability for learning. The author then states that speaking of the mosque is speaking of the main university for Islamic knowledge. Finally, he notes that study circles originated in mosques and persisted continuously across all regions.

Historical Mosques as Centers of Learning
Lists major mosques (Amr, Mansur, Damascus) as cultural centers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how early mosques served as comprehensive institutions for worship, education, judiciary, and community gathering, with examples from Cairo, Baghdad, and Damascus.

Core Idea
Mosques were the heart of Islamic civilization — centers for worship, learning, and governance.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid illustrates the expanded mission of mosques in early Islam. The author lists specific historical examples to show that mosques were not merely prayer halls but dynamic hubs of intellectual, judicial, and social activity. Understanding this pluralistic role helps us appreciate the depth of traditional Islamic education.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once to get the flow, then read the full English translation. Notice the list of mosque functions and the three examples.
The author uses a list structure: 'first, then, close to this date'.
2
Identify the mosques and their roles
Find the three mosques mentioned: Amr ibn al-As, al-Mansur, and Damascus. For each, note what the text says about its function or significance.
Pay attention to al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's wish as evidence for the Mosque of al-Mansur's scholarly status.
3
Reflect on the broader context
Consider how the mosques served as centers of learning beyond prayer. Which functions mentioned in the first sentence were exemplified by these mosques?
The passage begins with a general statement about the expanded mission.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد توسع المسلمون في عصورهم الأولى في فهم مهمة المسجد، فاتخذوه مكاناً للعبادة ومكاناً للتعليم، وداراً للقضاء، وساحة تتجمع فيها الجيوش، ومنزلاً لاستقبال السفراء. ومن أجل ذلك توسع المسلمون في بناء المساجد، فبنى عمرو بن العاص مسجده في القاهرة سنة ٢١ هـ، وفي عهد مبكر جداً جلس فيه سليمان بن عز التجيبي ليعظ الناس ويبصرهم بأمور دينهم ودنياهم . وفي عام ١٤٥ هـ بنى المنصور الخليفة العباسي مسجده فأصبح قبلة أنظار الأساتذة والطلاب في ذلك العهد.. ومما يدل على ذلك، أن الخطيب البغدادي لما حج شرب من ماء زمزم وسأل الله أن يحقق له ثلاث حاجات ، كان من بينها أن يتاح له أن يملي الحديث بجامع المنصور. وقريب من هذا التاريخ أنشىء مسجد دمشق، والذي كان يعد واحداً من عجائب الدنيا الأربع في ذلك العهد، وكان مركزاً هاماً من مراكز الثقافة في العالم الإسلامي
And the Muslims in their early centuries expanded in understanding the mission of the mosque, so they took it as a place of worship, a place of education, a house of judgment, a courtyard for gathering armies, and a lodging for receiving ambassadors. For that reason, the Muslims expanded in building mosques. 'Amr ibn al-'As built his mosque in Cairo in the year 21 AH, and in a very early era, Sulayman ibn 'Uzz al-Tujibi sat in it to preach to people and enlighten them about their religious and worldly affairs. And in the year 145 AH, al-Mansur, the 'Abbasid caliph, built his mosque, and it became the qibla of the eyes of professors and students in that era. Evidence of that is that al-Khatib al-Baghdadi, when he performed Hajj, drank from Zamzam water and asked Allah to grant him three wishes, among which was that he be allowed to dictate hadith in the Mosque of al-Mansur. And close to this date, the Mosque of Damascus was established, which was considered one of the four wonders of the world in that era, and it was an important center of culture in the Islamic world.
Source pages p. 85

Commentary
This passage illustrates the multifunctional role of mosques in early Islam. It begins by listing their expanded functions: worship, education, judiciary, military gathering, and diplomacy. Then it gives historical examples: the Mosque of 'Amr in Cairo (21 AH) where early preaching occurred; the Mosque of al-Mansur in Baghdad (145 AH) which attracted scholars, illustrated by al-Khatib al-Baghdadi's wish to teach there; and the Umayyad Mosque in Damascus, called one of the four wonders of the world and a major cultural hub.

Why Mosques Were Preferred for Teaching
Ibn Jubayr and al-Abdari explain the virtue of teaching in mosques.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical and methodological reasons why mosques were the preferred places for teaching in Islam.

Core Idea
The mosque is the best place to teach because it revives sunna, suppresses bid'a, and is accessible to all.

This passage draws on two historical sources—Ibn Jubayr's description of teaching circles in the Umayyad Mosque and al-Abdari's legal-methodological justification—to establish the mosque as the ideal venue for Islamic education. It highlights both the practical arrangements (stipends, dedicated corners) and the spiritual benefits.

Guided Path
1
Read the historical account by Ibn Jubayr
Read the passage about the Umayyad Mosque's teaching circles, stipends, and the Malikis' corner. Notice how organized and well-funded the teaching was.
Ibn Jubayr was a 12th-century traveler.
2
Learn about al-Azhar's founding
Read about the construction of al-Azhar Mosque in 360 AH and its transformation into a university in 378 AH. This exemplifies the mosque's role as a center of learning.
Al-Azhar remains a leading Islamic university today.
3
Study al-Abdari's justification
Al-Abdari lists three benefits of teaching in the mosque: manifesting a sunna, suppressing a bid'a, and teaching Allah's rulings. Consider why the mosque is uniquely suited for these.
Al-Abdari's work al-Madkhal is a classic on Islamic education methodology.
4
Reflect on accessibility
Notice that al-Abdari says the mosque is a place for all people—high and low, scholar and ignorant. This underscores the inclusive nature of mosque-based education.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
يحدثنا ابن جبير عنه فيقول : وفيه حلقات للتدريس للطلبة وللمدرسين فيها إجراء واسع وللمالكية زاوية للتدريس في الجانب الغربي يجتمع فيها الطلبة المغاربة ولهم إجراء معلوم .. إلخ . وفي سنة ٣٦٠هـ بنى جوهر الصقلي الجامع الأزهر، وقد خصص منذ سنة ٣٧٨هـ للدراسات والأبحاث العلمية، وظل من ذلك التاريخ حتى العهد الحاضر جامعة من الجامعات الأولى في العالم الإسلامي. ولقد فضل المسلمون المسجد على غيره ليكون مكاناً للعبادة وتلقي العلم . يقول العبدري في كتابه (المدخل): «أفضل مواضع التدريس هو المسجد لأن الجلوس للتدريس إنما فائدته أن تظهر به سنة، أو تخمد به بدعة، أو يتعلم به حكم من أحكام الله تعالى. والمسجد يحصل فيه هذا الغرض متوفراً ، لأنه موضع الناس رفيعهم ووضيعهم، عالمهم وجاهلهم»
Ibn Jubayr narrates about it saying: In it there are teaching circles for students and teachers, with ample stipend, and the Malikis have a teaching corner in the western side where Maghrebi students gather, and they have a known stipend... etc. And in the year 360 AH, Jawhar al-Siqilli built the Azhar mosque, and it was dedicated from the year 378 AH to academic studies and research, and it remained from that date until the present day a university among the first universities in the Islamic world. And the Muslims preferred the mosque over other places to be a place of worship and acquiring knowledge. Al-'Abdari says in his book 'al-Madkhal': 'The best place for teaching is the mosque, because the benefit of sitting for teaching is only that a sunna becomes manifest, or a bid'a is suppressed, or a ruling from Allah's rulings is learned. And the mosque attains this goal abundantly, because it is a place of people, their high and low, scholar and ignorant.'
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides evidence for the centrality of mosques in Islamic education. It starts with Ibn Jubayr's description of the Umayyad Mosque's teaching circles, stipends, and the Malikis' corner. Then it mentions the foundation of al-Azhar (360 AH) and its designation for studies (378 AH), which became a premier university. Finally, it quotes al-'Abdari's 'al-Madkhal' to justify this practice: the mosque is the best place for teaching because it revives sunna, suppresses innovation, teaches divine rulings, and is accessible to all people regardless of status.

Key Terms
حلقات التدريس
الجامع الأزهر
البدعة
السنة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Al-Abdari's statement is merely a personal preference or cultural custom, not a principled methodological point.
Correction: Al-Abdari is making a legal-methodological argument rooted in the objectives of teaching: reviving sunna, suppressing bid'a, and teaching divine rulings. The mosque's inclusivity enhances these goals.

Checkpoint

A) It is more comfortable.
B) It allows the teacher to charge fees.
C) It revives a sunna or suppresses a bid'a.
D) It attracts only scholars.

Key Takeaway
The mosque is the optimal place for teaching because it combines worship with learning, manifests sunna, and is accessible to all.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When was the last time you sat in a mosque specifically to learn something about your religion? If it's been a while, what is stopping you? This week, will you attend at least one class or halaqa at the mosque, even if just to listen?
?

?
The mosque is open to the high and low, scholar and ignorant. Is your heart open to learning from someone less educated than you, or teaching someone who knows less—even if they are from a different social background? What will you do this week to make your mosque's learning circles more inclusive?

Mosques and Madrasas: Cradles of Islamic Learning
Historical narrative with examples and a scholar's quote on mosque education.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how mosques evolved into multifunctional educational institutions and how madrasas later formalized the system.

Core Idea
Mosques were the first universities of Islam, blending worship with comprehensive education.

In early Islamic civilization, the mosque was far more than a place of prayer—it was the heart of community life, serving as a school, courthouse, and cultural center. This passage traces that legacy through famous mosques and the later rise of madrasas, showing how the tradition of learning was deeply embedded in sacred spaces.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow. Pay attention to the historical examples and the quote.
The first word 'انقطاع' may be a fragment—ignore it and focus on the substantive content.
2
Identify the roles of mosques
Notice the five roles listed: worship, education, judiciary, military assembly, and diplomatic reception. This shows the mosque’s centrality.
3
Examine the specific mosque examples
The passage gives four key mosques: Amr ibn al-As (Cairo), al-Mansur (Baghdad), Damascus, and al-Azhar. For each, note the date and educational significance.
Al-Azhar was founded in 360 AH and became a dedicated university from 378 AH.
4
Analyze al-'Abdari's quote
Read the quote from al-'Abdari explaining why mosques are the best teaching places. Understand the three benefits: manifesting sunna, suppressing bid'a, and teaching divine rulings.
The quote emphasizes accessibility for all social levels.
5
Reflect on the transition to madrasas
The passage ends with the Nizamiyya schools established by Nizam al-Mulk. Consider how this formalized the educational system while still building on the mosque tradition.
The final phrase 'وهي: الكتاب' likely refers to the curriculum or textbook.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
انقطاع ، وقد توسع المسلمون في عصورهم الأولى في فهم مهمة المسجد، فاتخذوه مكاناً للعبادة ومكاناً للتعليم، وداراً للقضاء، وساحة تتجمع فيها الجيوش، ومنزلاً لاستقبال السفراء. ومن أجل ذلك توسع المسلمون في بناء المساجد، فبنى عمرو بن العاص مسجده في القاهرة سنة ٢١ هـ، وفي عهد مبكر جداً جلس فيه سليمان بن عز التجيبي ليعظ الناس ويبصرهم بأمور دينهم ودنياهم . وفي عام ١٤٥ هـ بنى المنصور الخليفة العباسي مسجده فأصبح قبلة أنظار الأساتذة والطلاب في ذلك العهد.. ومما يدل على ذلك، أن الخطيب البغدادي لما حج شرب من ماء زمزم وسأل الله أن يحقق له ثلاث حاجات ، كان من بينها أن يتاح له أن يملي الحديث بجامع المنصور. وقريب من هذا التاريخ أنشىء مسجد دمشق، والذي كان يعد واحداً من عجائب الدنيا الأربع في ذلك العهد، وكان مركزاً هاماً من مراكز الثقافة في العالم الإسلامي . يحدثنا ابن جبير عنه فيقول : وفيه حلقات للتدريس للطلبة وللمدرسين فيها إجراء واسع وللمالكية زاوية للتدريس في الجانب الغربي يجتمع فيها الطلبة المغاربة ولهم إجراء معلوم .. إلخ . وفي سنة ٣٦٠هـ بنى جوهر الصقلي الجامع الأزهر، وقد خصص منذ سنة ٣٧٨هـ للدراسات والأبحاث العلمية، وظل من ذلك التاريخ حتى العهد الحاضر جامعة من الجامعات الأولى في العالم الإسلامي. ولقد فضل المسلمون المسجد على غيره ليكون مكاناً للعبادة وتلقي العلم . يقول العبدري في كتابه (المدخل): «أفضل مواضع التدريس هو المسجد لأن الجلوس للتدريس إنما فائدته أن تظهر به سنة، أو تخمد به بدعة، أو يتعلم به حكم من أحكام الله تعالى. والمسجد يحصل فيه هذا الغرض متوفراً ، لأنه موضع الناس رفيعهم ووضيعهم، عالمهم وجاهلهم». ثم كانت المدارس وكان إنشاؤها على يد الوزير نظام الملك وزر لألب أرسلان والملك شاه و نسبت هذه المدارس إلى منشئها نظام الملك فعرفه باسم المدارس النظامية وكانت غاية في الجلال والعظمة . ثم كانت كثيرة العدد شملت الأمصار والبلدان . وهي: الكتاب .
Interruption, and the Muslims expanded in their early periods in understanding the mission of the mosque, so they took it as a place for worship and a place for education, a house for judgment, a courtyard where armies gather, and a residence for receiving ambassadors. For that reason, the Muslims expanded in building mosques: Amr ibn al-As built his mosque in Cairo in 21 AH, and in a very early era Sulayman ibn Izz al-Tujibi sat there to preach to the people and enlighten them in matters of their religion and their worldly affairs. In the year 145 AH, al-Mansur, the Abbasid caliph, built his mosque, and it became the focus of attention of teachers and students in that era. An indication of that is that when al-Khatib al-Baghdadi performed Hajj, he drank from Zamzam water and asked God to grant him three wishes, among which was that he be able to dictate hadith in the Mosque of al-Mansur. Close to this date, the Mosque of Damascus was established, which was considered one of the four wonders of the world at that time, and was an important center of culture in the Islamic world. Ibn Jubayr tells us about it, saying: In it there are teaching circles for students and teachers with ample stipends, and for the Malikis there is a teaching corner on the western side where Maghrebi students gather and they have a known stipend, etc. In the year 360 AH, Jawhar al-Siqilli built the Jami' al-Azhar, and it was dedicated from the year 378 AH for scientific studies and research, and from that date to the present day it has remained a premier university in the Islamic world. The Muslims preferred the mosque over other places to be a place for worship and acquiring knowledge. Al-'Abdari says in his book al-Madkhal: 'The best places for teaching is the mosque, because the benefit of sitting for teaching is only that a sunna is manifested, or a bid'a is extinguished, or a ruling from God's rulings is learned. The mosque fulfills this purpose abundantly, because it is the gathering place of people, high and low, scholar and ignorant.' Then came the schools, and their establishment was by the hand of the vizier Nizam al-Mulk, who served as vizier for Alp Arslan and Malik Shah, and these schools were attributed to their founder Nizam al-Mulk, so they became known as the Nizamiyya schools, and they were of utmost grandeur and magnificence. Then they became numerous in number, encompassing cities and countries. And it is: the book.
Source pages p. 86

Commentary
This passage provides a historical overview of educational institutions in early Islamic civilization. It begins by describing the multifunctional role of mosques: not only for worship but also for education, judiciary, military assembly, and diplomatic reception. The author then gives specific examples: the Mosque of Amr ibn al-As in Cairo (21 AH), where early preaching occurred; the Mosque of al-Mansur in Baghdad (145 AH), which became a renowned center for hadith dictation; and the Umayyad Mosque in Damascus, counted among the wonders of the world and a major cultural hub. Ibn Jubayr's account highlights organized teaching circles and stipends for students. The founding of al-Azhar Mosque (360 AH) as a dedicated academic institution is noted, and a quote from al-'Abdari explains the preference for mosques in teaching: they are accessible to all social levels and serve to manifest sunna, suppress innovation, and teach divine rulings. The passage then transitions to the establishment of madrasas, specifically the Nizamiyya schools founded by Nizam al-Mulk, which became widespread and grand. The final line 'وهي: الكتاب' likely refers to the book (the curriculum) as the core educational tool, though the phrase is abrupt.

Key Terms
مسجد
مدارس نظامية
حلقات التدريس

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Islamic education in early times was completely informal with no structure.
Correction: The passage shows organized teaching circles with stipends, dedicated spaces for specific madhhabs, and later formal madrasas, indicating a structured system.

Checkpoint

A) Mosque of Amr ibn al-As
B) Mosque of al-Mansur
C) Jami' al-Azhar
D) Mosque of Damascus

Key Takeaway
Mosques were the original Islamic schools, and the later madrasa system built upon their tradition of accessible, comprehensive learning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
This passage shows that mosques were once vibrant centers of comprehensive education, not just for prayer. When you enter a mosque today, do you see it primarily as a place of worship only, or do you actively seek to gain knowledge there? What specific step will you take this week to attend or organize a learning circle in a mosque?
?

?
The Companions and early Muslims valued mosque study so much that al-Khatib al-Baghdadi prayed to dictate hadith in al-Mansur's mosque. Do you have a similar yearning to share Islamic knowledge? What is holding you back from teaching even one person? Will you commit to starting before this week ends?

The Establishment of the Nizamiyya Schools
Describes the founding and spread of the Nizamiyya schools by Nizam al-Mulk

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the historical significance of the Nizamiyya schools and their founder Nizam al-Mulk.

The Necessity of Teachers in Islamic Learning
Author argues for teacher-based learning via four authoritative quotes.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the traditional Islamic emphasis on receiving knowledge directly from qualified teachers and the criticisms of relying solely on books.

Core Idea
Knowledge without a teacher is like a ship without a captain.

This passage from Sharh al-Maqasid introduces a foundational principle of Islamic education: knowledge must be received from living teachers, not merely from texts. The author quotes several early authorities to stress that self-study from books alone leads to error and loss of religion. Understanding this principle helps us appreciate why classical scholars like Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani meticulously selected their teachers.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage slowly, then read the English translation. Notice the strong language used to warn against book-only learning.
The passage is short but packed with authoritative quotes.
2
Identify each transmitted quote
The passage contains four distinct sayings from Ibn Jama‘ah, the book al-Shakwa, Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr, and Imam al-Shafi‘i. Find each one and note the specific warning it gives.
These quotes form the core evidence for the author's point.
3
Understand why teachers are necessary
Reflect on the reasons given: oral transmission selects the best, books alone distort rulings, and without a teacher one follows Satan. Why is personal guidance so critical in religious sciences?
This connects to the broader theme of isnad (chain of transmission).
4
Connect to Taftazani's practice
The passage ends by stating that Taftazani chose his teachers carefully. How does this conclusion follow from the earlier warnings?
It shows that the principle was not just theory but practiced by great scholars.
5
Apply the lesson personally
Consider your own learning journey: How do you ensure you have qualified guidance? What are the dangers of relying solely on books or internet searches?
This reflection internalizes the lesson.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اهتم المسلمون اهتماماً كبيراً بتلقي العلم عن الأساتذة والمدرسين وكرهوا كراهة شديدة أن يتلقى الطالب العلم عن الكتب والقراطيس وحدها، وكان الشيخ الجليل ابن جماعة ( ۱ ) يقول : من أعظم البلية تشيخ الصحيفة. أي أن يتعلم الناس من الصحف والكتب وحدها. وورد في كتاب الشكوى (۲) من لا شيخ له فلا دين له. ومن لم يكن له . أستاذ فإمامه الشيطان، وروي عن مصعب بن الزبير رضي الله عنه أنه كان يقول : إن الناس يتحدثون بأحسن ما يحفظون، ويحفظون أحسن ما يكتبون ويكتبون أحسن ما يسمعون، فإذا أخذت الأدب فخذه من أفواه الرجال، فإنك لا تسمع إلا مختاراً، ولؤلؤاً منثوراً(۳). وروي عن الإمام الشافعي قوله : من تفقه من بطون الكتب، ضيع الأحكام (4) . من أجل ذلك اختار سعد الدين التفتازاني مجموعة من الأساتذة. ومن مشايخ عصره ممن كان يوثق بهم، ولهم بحث وطول اجتماع، وتقتضينا طبيعة البحث أن نلقي ضوءاً على بعض هؤلاء الرجال الأفذاذ.
The Muslims paid great attention to receiving knowledge from masters and teachers, and they intensely disliked that a student should receive knowledge from books and papers alone. The noble Shaykh Ibn Jama‘ah (1) used to say: 'Among the greatest afflictions is the shaykhdom of the page,' meaning that people learn from pages and books alone. It is mentioned in the book 'al-Shakwa' (2): 'He who has no shaykh has no religion; and he who has no teacher, his imam is Satan.' It is narrated from Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr, may Allah be pleased with him, that he used to say: 'People speak the best of what they memorize, memorize the best of what they write, and write the best of what they hear. So when you take adab, take it from the mouths of men, for you will hear only the chosen and scattered pearls.' (3) It is narrated from Imam al-Shafi‘i his saying: 'Whoever studies fiqh from the pages of books destroys the rulings.' (4) For this reason Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani chose a group of masters. And among the shaykhs of his time who were trustworthy, who had research and long association, and the nature of our research requires us to shed light on some of these outstanding men.
Source pages p. 90

Commentary
This passage is an introduction explaining why the author will discuss the teachers of Sa‘d al-Din al-Taftazani. It begins by stating the deep concern of Muslims for acquiring knowledge directly from teachers, and their strong disapproval of relying solely on books. The author then supports this with four transmitted quotes. First, Ibn Jama‘ah says that the worst calamity is for a person to set themselves up as a shaykh based only on reading books. Second, a book titled 'al-Shakwa' states that anyone without a shaykh has no religion, and that the devil is the imam of the one without a teacher. Third, Mus‘ab ibn al-Zubayr advises to take refinement (adab) from the mouths of learned men, because oral transmission selects the best. Fourth, Imam al-Shafi‘i warns that learning fiqh from books alone leads to neglecting proper rulings. The author concludes that because of this, Taftazani carefully selected a group of teachers from his era who were trustworthy and accomplished, and that it is appropriate to highlight some of these exceptional men.

Key Terms
تشيخ الصحيفة
لا شيخ له فلا دين له
الأدب

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Learning religious sciences entirely from books without a qualified teacher is acceptable and sufficient.
Correction: The passage shows that relying solely on books is considered a great affliction, leads to loss of religion, and destroys correct rulings. A teacher is essential for proper understanding and transmission.

Checkpoint

A) It is commendable.
B) It is the greatest affliction (تشيخ الصحيفة).
C) It is permissible only for adults.
D) It is better than learning from teachers.

Key Takeaway
Islamic tradition insists that true religious knowledge must be received from qualified teachers, not merely from books, because oral transmission preserves both content and context.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

?
The passage says that the best knowledge is heard from the mouths of men, not read from pages. What is one area of Islam you are currently learning from books or online that you could instead learn directly from a qualified teacher? What will you do about it today?

Editorial Methodology: Five Steps of Textual Criticism
Editor lists five methodological steps used in preparing the critical edition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the five methodological steps the editor used to prepare a reliable critical edition from multiple manuscripts.

Core Idea
Transparent editing preserves the author's intent.

Before engaging with the text itself, it is important to understand how the editor worked. This passage explains the steps taken to produce a clean, accurate edition while respecting the author's original structure. Knowing this methodology helps you trust the text and understand any editorial interventions.

Guided Path
1
Read the editorial passage
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice that the editor lists four main points marked (a)-(d).
The passage is straightforward, but pay attention to the details of each step.
2
Identify the five editorial actions
Even though there are four letters, step (d) includes two separate actions: explaining the book's structure and adding missing titles. Count them as five distinct editorial actions.
3
Understand the role of manuscript variants
Step (b) describes collating three copies and recording variants. This is the core of textual criticism: noting differences to recover the most authentic text.
The editor also mentions 'additions, omissions, and corruptions' in the printed copy.
4
Reflect on editorial transparency
The editor explains why new titles were added (the author often omitted them). This transparency allows readers to know where the editor has intervened.
Consider how this builds trust in the edition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثانياً : - ( أ ) قمنا بكتابة النسخة رقم (۱) على حسب قواعد الكتابة الحديثة مع إثبات علامات الترقيم أثناء الكتابة . (ب ) بعد الانتهاء من الكتابة أخذنا في مراجعة النسخة (أ) على النسخة (ب) و (ج) وأثبتنا بالهامش فروق النسخ الموجودة مع الإشارة إلى الزيادة والنقص والتحريفات في النسخة المطبوعة . (ج) حرصنا بقدر الطاقة على تنقية النص من الأخطاء النحوية واللغوية . ( د ) الكتاب قسمه المؤلف إلى مقاصد، والمقاصد إلى فصول، والفصول إلى مباحث. ولكنه أغفل في كثير من الأحيان أن يضع عناوين لكثير من الفصول والمباحث، فقمنا بوضع العناوين الملائمة لها وهناك بعض الموضوعات المتداخلة، والتي رأينا أنها في حاجة إلى عناوين مستقلة، وهذا ما حرصنا عليه .
Secondly: (a) We transcribed copy number (1) according to modern writing rules, with the insertion of punctuation marks during transcription. (b) After completing the transcription, we undertook revision of copy (a) against copies (b) and (c), and we recorded in the margin the variants found, with indication of additions, omissions, and corruptions in the printed copy. (c) We endeavored, to the best of our ability, to purify the text from grammatical and linguistic errors. (d) The author divided the book into maqāṣid (objectives), the maqāṣid into fuṣūl (chapters), and the fuṣūl into mabāḥith (investigations). But he often omitted to place titles for many chapters and investigations, so we placed appropriate titles for them; and there are some overlapping subjects for which we deemed they needed independent titles, and this is what we have done.
Source pages p. 146

Commentary
This passage outlines the editor's methodology. It is a list of five procedural steps. (a) They transcribed the base manuscript (copy 1) following modern orthographic conventions and added punctuation. (b) After transcription, they collated the new transcription against the other two manuscripts (b and c) and recorded variants in the margin, noting additions, omissions, and corruptions in the printed edition. (c) They meticulously corrected grammatical and linguistic errors. (d) They explain the original structure: the book is divided into maqāṣid, which are subdivided into fuṣūl, and then into mabāḥith. Since the author often left many sections without headings, the editor provided appropriate titles, especially for overlapping topics that required separate headings. This methodological transparency allows the reader to understand the nature of the editorial intervention.

Key Terms

فروق النسخ
الزيادة والنقص والتحريفات
الأخطاء النحوية واللغوية
المقاصد
الفصول
المباحث
عناوين مستقلة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the editor changed the author's text arbitrarily or censored it.
Correction: The editor only added titles where the author omitted them and corrected obvious errors; the original wording was preserved and variants were recorded transparently.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway
The editor's systematic methodology—transcription, collation, correction, and title supplementation—ensures textual accuracy while clarifying the author's intended structure.

Textual Variations in Classical Citations
Editor's report on textual fidelity of quotations.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the types of textual variations that occur in classical quotations and how editors trace them to original sources.

Core Idea
Not all quotes are verbatim; editors work to restore accuracy.

In classical scholarly works, authors often quote earlier sources, but not always with perfect fidelity. The editor of this text explains the different ways quotes were transmitted and his efforts to verify them. This lesson introduces the common textual issues and the editor's methodology for correction.

Guided Path
1
Identify the types of textual variation
The passage mentions quotations taken 'by meaning' vs 'verbatim', and those with additions, deletions, or corruptions. Understand each term and its significance.
These variations are common in handwritten transmission.
2
Understand the editor's response
The editor traced each quote to its original source when possible, and noted the book name and page number in the margin. This shows scholarly rigor.
The marginal notes are crucial for verifying the text.
3
Apply the lesson to your own study
Recognize that when you read classical works, you should be aware of possible textual variations. Always check critical editions and marginal notes.
This practice is part of the academic study of Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولقد وجدنا في كثير من النصوص التي أخذها من هذه الكتب أن بعضها أخذه بالمعنى، والبعض أخذه بالنص، وفي بعضها زيادات أو نقصان أو تحريفات . فعملنا بقدر الطاقة على نقلها من أصولها ، وأشرنا إلى ذلك في الهامش مع إثبات أسماء الكتب وتحديد الصفحات - ما أمكن ذلك - التي يوجد بها النص .
And we found in many of the texts that he took from these books that some he took by meaning, some he took verbatim, and in some there were additions, deletions, or corruptions. So we endeavored within our capacity to transmit them from their originals, and we pointed that out in the margin with the mention of book names and specification of pages—as much as possible—in which the text is found.
Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage is the editor's observation on the textual fidelity of the quotations from the sources. The editor notes that many of the texts taken by the commentator from these books were not always literal. Some were taken by meaning (بالمعنى), meaning the commentator paraphrased. Others were taken verbatim (بالنص). In some cases, there were additions, deletions, or corruptions (تحريفات). This is a common issue in classical texts. The editor then describes his methodology: he tried as much as possible to trace these quotes back to their original sources, and he indicated his findings in the margins, including the names of the books and the page numbers where the original text can be found. This shows scholarly diligence in verifying sources.

Key Terms
بالمعنى
بالنص
زيادات
نقصان
تحريفات
الهامش

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: تحريفات means intentional distortion or falsification.
Correction: Here تحريفات refers to unintentional textual corruptions in transmission, not deliberate tampering.

Checkpoint

The editor's diligence in tracing quotes to their original sources is a model of scholarly verification.

Editor's Correction of Corrupted Scholar Names
Editor's note on correcting corrupted names

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's methodology in correcting corrupted names and providing biographical references.

Core Idea
Reliable editions require correcting names and providing biographies.

In critical editions of classical texts, one of the editor's key tasks is to restore corrupted names. This short note from the editor of Sharh al-Maqasid explains his approach to identifying and correcting such names, and why he adds biographical information. Understanding this editorial practice helps you appreciate the scholarly rigor behind the text you are studying.

Guided Path
1
Read the editor's note
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully to understand the editor's claim.
2
Understand 'corrupted names'
The word 'محرفة' means names that have been distorted or incorrectly transmitted. Consider why this happens in manuscripts.
3
Identify the corrective actions
The editor did two things: corrected the names and provided translations (biographies) from primary sources.
4
Appreciate the purpose
The goal is to help the reader identify the scholars and consult further sources if needed.
5
Note the key term 'الترجمة'
Here it means 'biography', not 'translation'. This is a common misunderstanding.

Editor enumerates four specific books of Ibn Sina used by the author.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand which specific works of Ibn Sina the author of Sharh al-Maqasid relied upon and why these sources are significant.

Core Idea
The author heavily used Ibn Sina's philosophical and medical works.

In this passage, the editor of Sharh al-Maqasid lists the exact books of Ibn Sina that the author consulted. This is important because it shows the influence of Avicennan philosophy on Islamic theology. The list includes major works like al-Shifa and al-Isharat. Understanding this helps us see the intellectual sources behind the text.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully

Notice that the third book is missing a numeral; likely '3' before Kitab al-Qanun.
2
Identify the four books
The passage presents four books. Find each one and note their full titles and any specific sections mentioned (e.g., al-Shifa's section on dialectic and logic).
Keep in mind that these are works of Ibn Sina, a major philosopher and physician.
3
Consider the topics where these books were used
The editor notes that the author used these books especially when writing the fourth and fifth aims (maqasid). The topics include inner senses, abstract entities, celestial and human souls, etc. Reflect on why these philosophical works would be relevant to those discussions.
This shows the integration of peripatetic philosophy into Islamic theological discourse.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Source pages p. 148

Commentary
This passage lists the specific books of Ibn Sina that the author of Sharh al-Maqasid relied upon. This shows the extensive use of Ibn Sina's philosophical and medical works.

Editor's Method for Tracing Sects and Poetry
Editor outlines two final editorial tasks.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the editor's dual approach to identifying sources for Islamic sects and poems in the commentary.

Core Idea
The editor's notes trace the author's sources for sects and poetry.

This passage reveals the final steps of the editor's methodology: locating the original statements of Islamic sects and attributing the poems cited by the author. These tasks demonstrate the editor's commitment to providing a critical apparatus.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once to grasp the two numbered tasks. Then read the translation.
2
Identify the two tasks
Notice that the editor indicates two separate editorial actions: one concerning sectarian statements (النقول) and one concerning poetic lines (الأشعار).
These are numbered 'Eighthly' and 'Ninthly'.
3
Understand the rationale
Consider why locating the original sources enhances the scholarly value of the edition. It allows readers to verify claims and see the author's engagement with primary texts.
4
Reflect on common misunderstanding
Many readers might think the editor only worked on sects. This passage shows poetry was also handled. Check the misconception question to clarify.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثامناً : ناقش المؤلف الفرق الإسلامية، معتمداً على بعض نقولهم فأشرنا بقدر الطاقة إلى أماكن هذه النقول . تاسعاً : نسبنا الأشعار التي استشهد بها المؤلف إلى قائلها وأرشدنا إلى بعض المراجع التي توجد بها .
Eighthly: The author discussed Islamic sects, relying on some of their statements, so we indicated as much as possible the places of these statements. Ninthly: We attributed the poems that the author cited to their speakers and directed to some references in which they are found.
Source pages p. 149

Commentary
Two final editorial tasks are described. First, for the author's discussions of Islamic sects, the editor attempted to locate the original sources of the sectarian positions mentioned. Second, for the poetic quotations scattered throughout the work, the editor identified the poets and provided references to where the poems can be found. This enhances the scholarly value of the edition by tracing the author's sources.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

The Book's Six Maqasid and Human Faculties
Author states six maqasid; commentator begins defining via human faculties.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the book is divided into six overarching objectives (maqasid) and that the commentator begins to define them by referencing the theoretical and practical faculties of humans.

Core Idea
The book's six maqasid are rooted in human theoretical and practical faculties.

This lesson introduces the structural framework of the book: it is organized into six maqasid (objectives). The commentator begins to unpack this by pointing to two fundamental human faculties—theoretical and practical—which will be key to understanding the maqasid. Even though the passage is incomplete, it sets the stage for the deeper discussion that follows.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the author's statement and the commentator's interjection.
The passage ends abruptly; note what is missing.
2
Identify the six maqasid structure
The author explicitly states the book is arranged upon six maqasid. This is the foundational organizational claim.
The commentator is about to explain what 'maqasid' means here.
3
Understand the commentator's approach
The commentator begins with human faculties: theoretical and practical. This suggests that the maqasid relate to perfecting knowledge and action.
The passage cuts off before the full explanation.
4
Reflect on the incomplete passage
Consider that the commentator's definition is incomplete. What might he have said next? This reminds us that texts often require careful study and reference to other sources.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال: ورتبته على ستة مقاصد. أقول: اعلم أن للإنسان قوة نظرية كما لها معرفة الحقائق كما هي، وعملية كمالها
He said: And he arranged it upon six objectives (maqaasid). I say: Know that man has a theoretical faculty... likewise... knowledge of realities as they are, and a practical faculty... its perfection.
Source pages p. 157

Commentary
This passage presents the book's structural organization and the commentator's initial explanation. It begins with the statement 'قال: ورتبته على ستة مقاصد' (He said: And he arranged it upon six maqasid). This is likely a quotation from the original author (the author of the matn), indicating that the book is divided into six major objectives, or maqasid. The commentator then interjects with 'أقول' (I say), signaling his own commentary. He begins to define the term 'maqasid' by discussing human faculties: 'اعلم أن للإنسان قوة نظرية كما لها معرفة الحقائق كما هي، وعملية كمالها' (Know that man has a theoretical faculty... likewise... knowledge of realities as they are, and a practical faculty... its perfection). The commentator seems to be setting up a definition of the maqasid in terms of these two faculties, suggesting that the book's objectives relate to the perfection of theoretical and practical knowledge. However, the text breaks off before completing the explanation, so the full definition is not provided in this passage.

The Five Chapters and Six Objectives of Ilm al-Kalam
Enumeration of chapters and explanation of maqasid arrangement.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the discipline of kalam is structured into five main chapters and six objectives, and why this organization is logically necessary.

Core Idea
The chapters of kalam mirror the hierarchy of existence: general, substance, accident, divine, and revealed.

The author begins by defining the scope of kalam: knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, actions, prophethood, and the afterlife, plus matters beyond independent reason. From this domain, he derives five essential chapters. Understanding this classification is key to grasping the entire theological project, as it determines how topics are arranged and why some discussions appear in specific sections.

Guided Path
1
Identify the scope of kalam
Read the opening sentence to see what kalam covers: knowledge of Allah, His attributes, actions, prophethood, and the afterlife, as well as matters inaccessible to independent reason and the states of substances and accidents.
This scope justifies the chapters that follow.
2
Memorize the five chapters
Locate the list: 1) General Matters, 2) Accidents, 3) Substances, 4) Divine Matters, 5) Revealed Matters. Understand that 'General Matters' is a preface covering overarching concepts.
These five are the backbone of classical kalam manuals.
3
Explore the six maqasid
The author then shows how these chapters map to six objectives: first (principles), second (general matters), third (accidents), fourth (substances), fifth (divine), sixth (revealed). Notice the logic: topics not specific to one category come second, specific ones in order of ontological precedence.
Maqasid 1 covers introductory material not part of the five core chapters.
4
Understand the rationale for ordering
The author explains that later topics depend on earlier ones in some proofs, and that sometimes a topic from a later chapter is placed earlier for coherence (e.g., vision in divine matters).
This reveals the pedagogical concern behind the arrangement.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاقتصر المليون (۱) على ما يتعلق بمعرفة الصانع وصفاته وأفعاله وما يتفرع على ذلك من النبوة والمعاد. وسائر مالا سبيل (۲) للعقل باستقلاله ، وما يترتب عليه إثبات ذلك من الأحوال المختصة بالجواهر والأعراض أو الشاملة لأكثر الموجودات فجاءت أبواب الكلام خمسة هي : الأمور العامة والأعراض والجواهر، والإلهيات والسمعيات . وقد جرت العادة بتصديرها بمباحث تجري مجرى السوابق لها تسمى بالمبادىء فرتبنا الكتاب على ستة مقاصد ، ووجه الضبط أن المذكور (۳) فيه إن كان من مقاصد الكلام فإما سمعيات وهو المقصد السادس، أو عقليات مختص بالواجب وهو الخامس، أو بالممكن الجوهر وهو الرابع ، أو العرض وهو الثالث، أو لا مختص (٤) بواحد وهو الثاني ، وإن لم يكن من مقاصد الفن فهو المقصد الأول من الكتاب، ووجه الترتيب توقف اللاحق على السابق، في بعض البينات (٥) ، وقد يقتضي الضبط والمناسبة إيراد شيء من مباحث تأتي (٦) في الآخر كمسألة الرؤية في الإلهيات، وإعادة المعدوم في السمعيات . (۱) في (ب) الملبون وهو تحريف . (۲) زيد في (ب) كلمة (إليه). (۳) سقطت من (ب) كلمة (أن) . (٤) في (ب) أو لا يختص . (٥) في (ب) البيانات . (٦) في (ب) باب في آخر .
The Muslims (al-milliyyun) limited themselves to what pertains to knowledge of the Maker, His attributes, and His actions, and what branches from that: prophethood and the afterlife. And everything that the intellect has no independent way to [know], and what entails proof of that from the states specific to substances and accidents or encompassing most existents. Thus the chapters of kalam (abwab al-kalam) became five: General Matters (al-umur al-amma), Accidents (al-a'rad), Substances (al-jawahir), Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat), and Revealed Matters (al-sam'iyyat). And the custom has been to precede them with investigations that serve as preliminaries called 'the principles' (al-mabadi'). So we arranged the book into six objectives (maqasid). And the rationale for this organization is that whatever is mentioned in it, if it is from the objectives of kalam, then either it is revealed matters (sam'iyyat), which is the sixth objective; or rational matters specific to the Necessary Being (al-wajib), which is the fifth; or to the possible substance (al-mumkin al-jawhar), which is the fourth; or to the accident (al-'arad), which is the third; or not specific to any one, which is the second. And if it is not from the objectives of the art, then it is the first objective of the book. And the rationale for the order is the dependence of the later on the earlier in some proofs. And sometimes organization and suitability require including some topics that come later in the earlier sections, like the issue of vision (ru'ya) in Divine Matters, and the re-creation of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in Revealed Matters. (1) In copy (ب): 'al-mullabun' and it is a distortion. (2) In copy (ب): added the word (إليه). (3) In copy (ب): the word (أن) is missing. (4) In copy (ب): 'or not specific' (or 'or does not specialize'). (5) In copy (ب): 'al-bayanat' (the proofs? instead of 'al-binat'). (6) In copy (ب): 'a chapter in the last'.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage defines the scope and structure of kalam as a discipline. The author begins by noting that Muslim scholars (al-milliyyun) focused their efforts on knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, and His actions, along with derivative topics like prophethood and the afterlife. The domain of kalam also includes matters that the intellect cannot independently grasp and the evidentiary states pertaining to substances and accidents. From this, the author enumerates the five chapters (abwab) of kalam: (1) General Matters (al-umum al-amma), (2) Accidents (al-a'rad), (3) Substances (al-jawahir), (4) Divine Matters (al-ilahiyyat), and (5) Revealed Matters (al-sam'iyyat). These five core chapters are typically preceded by introductory investigations called al-mabadi' (the principles). The author then explains how he arranged his own book, Sharh al-Maqasid, into six objectives (maqasid), corresponding to these divisions. The first maqasid covers introductory material not part of kalam's core objectives. The second maqasid covers general matters that are not specific to any one category. The third covers accidents, the fourth covers possible substances, the fifth covers the Necessary Being, and the sixth covers revealed matters. This ordering is logical because later topics depend on earlier ones in some proofs. The author also notes that sometimes it is necessary to include a topic from a later section in an earlier one for coherence, such as the discussion of the beatific vision (ru'ya) in the divine section or the restoration of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in the revealed section. The passage ends with textual notes that record variant readings from different manuscripts, indicating the author's scholarly care in establishing the text.

Key Terms

الأمور العامة
الأعراض

السمعيات
المبادىء
المقاصد الستة
الواجب
الممكن
الرؤية
إعادة المعدوم

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The arrangement of kalam topics into chapters and objectives is arbitrary and simply a convention.
Correction: The passage shows that the structure derives from the subject matter itself: general, specific to substances/accidents, divine, and revealed. Moreover, ordering follows logical dependence and pedagogical suitability, making it a principled classification.

Checkpoint
How many main chapters (abwab) of kalam does the author mention?
A) 3
B) 4
C) 5
D) 6

Key Takeaway
The five chapters and six objectives of kalam are not arbitrary but derived logically from the scope of the discipline and the relationship between topics.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn about Allah and His attributes, do you ever find yourself jumping to conclusions about details without first understanding the broad principles that underlie them? How might the logical order of topics—from general to specific, and from rational to revealed—change the way you approach your own study of creed? This lesson shows that the order is not arbitrary but based on dependency. Take a moment to identify one area of Islamic belief where you have studied a sub-topic without fully grasping its foundational principle. Commit to returning to that foundation this week.
?
The author notes that sometimes later topics are included earlier for coherence, like the vision of Allah in Divine Matters. In your own spiritual life, have you ever needed a glimpse of a future hope (like the Beatific Vision) to make sense of a present difficulty? How does this anticipation shape your worship and patience? This lesson reminds us that even in systematic theology, some truths are woven in early because we need them. What specific truth from the Hereafter do you need to carry with you into your daily routine as a light for your current path?

The Six Maqasid of Sharh al-Maqasid: Structure and Rationale
Taftazani explains the arrangement of his book's six maqasid and the rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Taftazani organizes his book into six maqasid, and the reasoning behind the order of these sections.

Core Idea
The six maqasid are arranged by logical dependence: preliminaries, then general, then specific, then revelational.

Taftazani based the structure of Sharh al-Maqasid on the traditional five chapters of kalam, but reorganized them into six maqasid with a clear rationale. In this passage, he explains how each maqsad relates to the subject matter of kalam and why they appear in that order. Understanding this framework helps you navigate the entire book.

Guided Path
1
Identify the traditional five chapters
Read the passage and note the five chapters that traditionally constitute kalam: universal matters, accidents, substances, divine things, and revelational matters.
Taftazani mentions they are prefaced by 'principles' (mabadi).
2
Map the six maqasid to the chapters
The six maqasid are: 1) preliminaries (not part of kalam proper), 2) universal matters, 3) accidents, 4) substances, 5) divine things, 6) revelational matters. See how the first is an introduction, and the rest correspond to the five chapters.
The second maqsad covers what is not specific to any one category.
3
Understand the ordering rationale
The order is based on dependence: later maqasid depend on earlier ones in proofs. Also note that sometimes later topics are mentioned earlier for appropriateness, e.g., vision of God in divine things.
This shows Taftazani's methodological flexibility.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فاقتصر المليون على ما يتعلق بمعرفة الصانع وصفاته وأفعاله وما يتفرع على ذلك من النبوة والمعاد. وسائر ما لا سبيل للعقل باستقلاله، وما يترتب عليه إثبات ذلك من الأحوال المختصة بالجواهر والأعراض أو الشاملة لأكثر الموجودات فجاءت أبواب الكلام خمسة هي: الأمور العامة والأعراض والجواهر، والإلهيات والسمعيات. وقد جرت العادة بتصديرها بمباحث تجري مجرى السوابق لها تسمى بالمبادئ فرتبنا الكتاب على ستة مقاصد، ووجه الضبط أن المذكور فيه إن كان من مقاصد الكلام فإما سمعيات وهو المقصد السادس، أو عقليات مختص بالواجب وهو الخامس، أو بالممكن الجوهر وهو الرابع، أو العرض وهو الثالث، أو لا مختص بواحد وهو الثاني، وإن لم يكن من مقاصد الفن فهو المقصد الأول من الكتاب، ووجه الترتيب توقف اللاحق على السابق، في بعض البينات، وقد يقتضي الضبط والمناسبة إيراد شيء من مباحث تأتي في الآخر كمسألة الرؤية في الإلهيات، وإعادة المعدوم في السمعيات.
The [؟] confined themselves to what relates to knowledge of the Maker, His attributes, His actions, and what branches from that of prophethood and the afterlife. And the rest of what the intellect has no independent way to [know], and what depends on establishing that from the states specific to substances and accidents or common to most existents, so the chapters of kalam became five: universal matters, accidents, substances, divine things, and revelational matters. And custom has been to preface them with discussions that serve as preliminaries for them, called the principles. So we arranged the book into six maqasid. And the manner of organization is that what is mentioned therein, if it is from the maqasid of kalam, then either it is revelational matters, which is the sixth maqsad, or rational matters: specific to the Necessary, which is the fifth; or to the possible: the substance, which is the fourth; or the accident, which is the third; or not specific to one, which is the second. And if it is not from the maqasid of the art, then it is the first maqsad of the book. And the manner of ordering is the dependence of the later on the earlier in some of the proofs. And organization and appropriateness may require bringing forward something from discussions that come later, such as the issue of vision in divine things, and the restoration of the non-existent in revelational matters.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This passage is a methodological explanation of how the author, Taftazani, structured his book Sharh al-Maqasid. It begins by noting that earlier scholars ( perhaps 'the later ones' but the text has 'المليون' which is unclear) confined themselves to topics concerning knowledge of the Creator, His attributes, and His actions, along with what branches from that, namely prophethood and the afterlife, and other matters beyond the independent reach of reason. From these topics, the traditional five chapters of kalam emerged: universal matters (al-umur al-'amma), accidents (al-a'rad), substances (al-jawahir), divine things (al-ilahiyat), and revelational matters (al-sam'iyyat). Customarily, these chapters are preceded by introductory discussions called the principles (al-mabadi'). The author then states he arranged his book into six maqasid (goals or sections). He explains the logical organization: anything that belongs to the maqasid of kalam is either sam'iyyat (the sixth maqsad) or rational. The rational is further divided by what it is specific to: if specific to the Necessary (al-wajib), it is the fifth maqsad; if specific to a possible substance, it is the fourth; if specific to a possible accident, it is the third; if not specific to any one of these, it is the second. Anything that does not belong to the subject matter of kalam at all is placed in the first maqsad, which serves as an introduction. The order of these maqasid is based on the dependence of later material on earlier material in some proofs. However, decorum and appropriateness may sometimes require presenting a topic that is later, such as discussing the vision of God (al-ru'ya) in the chapter on divine things, or the restoration of the non-existent (i'adat al-ma'dum) in the chapter on revelational matters, even though these are logically later.

Key Terms
مقاصد
أمور عامة
أعراض
جواهر
إلهيات
سمعيات
مبادئ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All six maqasid are equally part of kalam's subject matter.
Correction: The first maqsad (preliminaries) is not part of kalam proper but an introduction; only the second through sixth belong to kalam's subject matter.

Checkpoint
How many maqasid does Taftazani divide his book into, and which maqsad covers revelational matters?
A) Five maqasid, with revelational matters as the fifth
B) Six maqasid, with revelational matters as the sixth
C) Six maqasid, with revelational matters as the third
D) Five maqasid, with revelational matters as the second

Key Takeaway
Taftazani's six maqasid are logically ordered so that later sections build on earlier ones, with the first serving as an introduction to the subject.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Taftazani carefully ordered his maqasid so that later ones depend on earlier ones. In your own learning of Islamic theology, have you ever skipped a foundational topic and later found yourself confused? What step can you take today to ensure you build your knowledge on a proper foundation?
?
The first maqsad is not part of kalam proper but serves as a necessary preliminary. Are there areas in your life where you have jumped into advanced discussions before mastering the basics? How can you humble yourself to start at the beginning even if it feels 'beneath' you?
?
Taftazani mentions that sometimes decorum requires bringing forward a topic that logically belongs later. When have you felt pressured to address a difficult question out of order, perhaps to satisfy others' expectations? How can you balance intellectual honesty with the need to maintain proper sequence in your understanding?

Structure of the First Maqsad: Principles and Chapters
Sanusi explains the division of the first maqsad into three chapters and the rationale.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the first maqsad of the book is structured into three chapters and why this division reflects the two types of principles: general preliminaries and kalam-specific topics.

Core Idea
The first maqsad splits into three chapters for two kinds of principles: universal preliminaries and kalam-specific investigations.

This lesson opens the first major aim (maqsad) of the book, which deals with the foundational principles of kalam. The author, al-Sanusi, explains that these principles fall into two categories: those that preface every science (definition, subject, goal) and those that are particular to the science of kalam (knowledge and reasoning). He devotes one chapter to the former and two chapters to the latter, totalling three chapters. Understanding this structure helps us see how kalam builds its method.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation to grasp the author's explanation of the three-chapter division.
2
Identify the two types of principles
Notice that the author distinguishes between general preliminaries (definition, subject, goal) common to all sciences and kalam-specific topics (knowledge and reasoning).
3
Understand the rationale
The author explains that kalam-specific topics are needed because acquiring beliefs through reasoning and refuting deniers depends on them, and no other Islamic science is more fitting to explain them.
4
Reflect on the structure
Consider why the author placed the general preliminaries in one chapter and the kalam-specific topics in two chapters, making three total.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول : في المبادىء. وفيه فصول ثلاثة(۱). الأول في المقدمات (۲)) . الفصل الأول في المقدمات أقول : رتبته (۳) على ثلاثة فصول لأن المبادىء منها ما رأوا تصدير كل (٤) علم بها كمعرفة حده وموضوعه وغايته (٥) ونحو ذلك فسماها ، بالمقدمات، وجعلها في فصل ومنها ما صدروا بها علم الكلام خاصة كمباحث العلم والنظر، لأن تحصيل العقائد بطريق النظر والاستدلال والرد على منكري حصول العلم أصلا، واستفادته من النظر مطلقاً ، أو في الإلهيات خاصة يتوقف على ذلك، وليس في العلوم الإسلامية ما هو أليق ببيانه فجعلها في فصلين.
The first aim: concerning the principles. It contains three chapters. The first is on the preliminaries. The first chapter: on the preliminaries. I say: He arranged it into three chapters because among the principles are those that they saw to preface every science with, such as knowing its definition, subject, and goal and the like, so they called them 'preliminaries' and placed them in one chapter. And among them are those with which they prefaced specifically the science of kalam, such as the investigations of knowledge and reasoning, because the acquisition of beliefs by way of reasoning and inference and the refutation of those who deny the attainment of knowledge at all, and its derivation from reasoning absolutely, or specifically in divine matters, depends on that. And there is no Islamic science more fitting to explain it, so he placed them in two chapters.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage explains the structure of the first maqsad (aim) of the book, which is about the principles (al-mabadi'). The author states that the first maqsad contains three chapters. He then explains that principles are of two types: general principles that preface every science, such as its definition (hadd), subject (mawdu'), and goal (ghaya), which are called 'preliminaries' (al-muqaddimat) and are placed in the first chapter. The second type consists of principles specific to the science of kalam, namely the investigations of knowledge (al-ilm) and reasoning (al-nazar), because the acquisition of beliefs through reasoning and the refutation of those who deny knowledge or its derivation from reasoning depend on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more appropriate for explaining these topics, the author devotes two chapters to them, making a total of three chapters. The logic is that the general preliminaries are common to all sciences, while the specific ones are needed for kalam's method.

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Preliminaries only refer to the definition of a science.
Correction: Preliminaries include the definition, subject, and goal of a science, as well as other introductory matters.

Checkpoint
Why are the investigations of knowledge and reasoning placed in separate chapters in the first maqsad?
A) They are general to all sciences.
B) They are specific to kalam and needed for its method.
C) They are unrelated to kalam.
D) They are part of logic only.

Key Takeaway
The first maqsad of the book is structured into three chapters: one for general preliminaries and two for kalam-specific investigations of knowledge and reasoning.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

?

?

Do you treat theology as the foundation of your intellectual life, or as a secondary interest? If it is not central, what change will you make starting this week to give it the priority it deserves?
