The Arrangement of the First Objective's Three Chapters
The author explains why he divided the first objective into three chapters.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the author divided the first objective into three chapters based on the nature of preliminaries.

Core Idea
The three chapters mirror two types of preliminaries: general and kalām-specific.

This lesson explores the author's rationale for structuring the opening chapters of his work. Before diving into the specific doctrines of kalām, he explains that certain foundational concepts must be addressed first. Some of these are common to all sciences, while others are unique to kalām. Let's see how he organizes them.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses a very logical structure.
2
Identify the two types of preliminaries
The passage distinguishes between general introductory matters (like definition, subject, purpose) and kalām-specific topics (knowledge and reasoning). Find where each is mentioned.
The general ones are called 'muhimmat' and the specific ones are 'mabāhis al-ilm wa al-nazar'.
3
Understand why two chapters for kalām-specific topics
The author argues that the acquisition of beliefs by reasoning, and refutation of deniers, depends on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more fitting, he dedicates two separate chapters.
This shows the central role of epistemology in kalām.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الأول: في المبادىء. وفيه فصول ثلاثة. الأول في المقدمات. الفصل الأول في المقدمات. أقول: رتبته على ثلاثة فصول لأن المبادىء منها ما رأوا تصدير كل علم بها كمعرفة حده وموضوعه وغايته ونحو ذلك فسماها بالمقدمات، وجعلها في فصل ومنها ما صدروا بها علم الكلام خاصة كمباحث العلم والنظر، لأن تحصيل العقائد بطريق النظر والاستدلال والرد على منكري حصول العلم أصلا، واستفادته من النظر مطلقاً، أو في الإلهيات خاصة يتوقف على ذلك، وليس في العلوم الإسلامية ما هو أليق ببيانه فجعلها في فصلين.
The first objective: concerning the preliminaries. And it contains three chapters. The first is on the introductory matters. The first chapter: on the introductory matters. I say: I arranged it in three chapters because among the preliminaries are those which they saw as making every science begin with them, such as knowing its definition, subject matter, and purpose and the like, so they called them 'introductory matters' and placed them in one chapter; and among them are those with which they began the science of kalām specifically, such as the investigations of knowledge and reasoning, because the acquisition of beliefs through the method of reasoning and inference, and the refutation of those who deny the attainment of knowledge at all, and its benefit from reasoning absolutely, or in the divine matters specifically, depends on that, and there is no Islamic science more fitting for its exposition, so he placed them in two chapters.
Source pages p. 163

Commentary
This passage presents the author's rationale for structuring the first objective (al-maqṣad al-awwal) of his work into three chapters. He explains that the preliminaries (mabādiʾ) are of two types. The first type consists of those preliminary matters that every science begins with, such as the definition, subject matter, and purpose of the science. These are called 'introductory matters' (muqaddimāt) and are placed in one chapter. The second type consists of those preliminaries that are specific to the science of kalām, namely the investigations of knowledge (al-ʿilm) and reasoning (al-naẓar). The author notes that acquiring beliefs through reasoning and inference, as well as refuting those who deny the possibility of knowledge or its derivation from reasoning (whether absolutely or specifically in divine matters), depends on these investigations. Since no other Islamic science is more appropriate for explaining these topics, he dedicates two separate chapters to them—one on knowledge and one on reasoning. Thus the three chapters cover: general introductory matters, knowledge, and reasoning.

The Subject Defines and Unifies a Science
Al-Taftazani argues for the subject as the basis of scientific distinction and unity.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Taftazani argues that the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues.

Core Idea
The subject is the heart of every science—it gives identity and holds the issues together.

In this opening part of his discussion, al-Taftazani states the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects. He then explains why it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject: it provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and it reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, guiding the student to what belongs and what does not. This principle establishes the subject as the primary unifying factor for the issues of a science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation. Focus on the key claim that the subject is the aspect of unity for issues.
Notice the logical flow from consensus to verification.
2
Identify the main argument
Find where al-Taftazani states that the subject is the aspect of unity for issues of a science. Understand why he emphasizes this.
He says: 'There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject.'
3
Understand the process of scientific inquiry
He describes how scholars study realities, obtain propositions (issues), and group them under subjects. See how this leads to the formation of distinct sciences.
The subject can be the reality itself, a part, a type, or an essential accident.
4
Reflect on secondary aspects
He acknowledges that definitions and purposes also provide unity but emphasizes that the subject is primary in essence.
The subject is the basis for differentiation in themselves, even if definitions serve for the student.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أقول : اتفقت كلمة القوم على أن التمييز العلوم في أنفسها إنما هو بحسب تمايز الموضوعات فناسب تصدير العلم ببيان الموضوع لإفادة لما به يتميز بحسب الذات بعد ما أفاد التعريف التمييز بحسب المفهوم، وأيضاً في معرفة جهة الوحدة للكثرة المطلوبة إحاطة بها إجمالاً بحيث إذا قصد تحصيل تفاصيلها لم ينصرف الطلب عما هو منها إلى ما ليس منها، ولا شك أن جهة وحدة مسائل العلم أولاً وبالذات هو الموضوع إذ فيه اشتراكها وبه اتحادها على ما سنفصله، وتحقيق المقام أنهم لما حاولوا معرفة أحوال الأشياء بقدر الطاقة البشرية على ما هو المراد بالحكمة وصنعوا الحقائق أنواعاً وأجناساً وغيرها، كالإنسان والحيوان والموجود وبحثوا عن أحوالها المختصة وأثبتوها لها بالأدلة فحصلت لهم قضايا كسبية محمولاتها أعراض ذاتية لتلك الحقائق، سموها بالمسائل، وجعلوا كل طائفة منها يرجع إلى واحد من تلك الأشياء بأن تكون موضوعاتها نفسه أو جزءاً له أو نوعاً منه أو عرضاً ذاتياً له علماً خاصاً يفرد بالتدوين والتسمية والتعليم نظراً إلى ما لتلك الطائفة على كثرتها، واختلاف محمولاتها من الاتحاد من جهة الموضوع أي الاشتراك فيه على الوجه المذكور ثم قد يتحد من جهات أخرى كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذ لها من بعض تلك الجهات ما يفيد تصورها إجمالاً، ومن حيث إن لها وحدة فيكون حداً للعلم إن دل على حقيقة مسماه أعني ذلك المركب الاعتباري كما يقال: هو علم يبحث فيه عن كذا أو علم بقواعد كذا، وإلا فرسماً كما يقال هو علم يقتدر به على كذا أو يحترز عن كذا، أو يكون آلة لكذا، فظهر أن الموضوع هو جهة وحدة مسائل العلم الواحد نظراً إلى ذاتها وإن عرضت لها جهات أخر كالتعريف والغاية، فإنه لا معنى لكون هذا علماً وذاك علماً آخر سوى أنه يبحث هذا عن أحوال شيء وذلك عن أحوال شيء آخر مغاير له بالذات أو بالاعتبار، فلا يكون تمايز العلوم في أنفسها، وبالنظر إلى ذواتها إلا بحسب الموضوع، وإن كانت تتمايز عند الطالب بما لها من التعريفات والغايات ونحوهما، ولهذا جعلوا تباين العلوم وتناسبها وتداخلها أيضاً بحسب الموضوع، بمعنى أن موضوع أحد العلمين إن كان مبايناً لموضوع الآخر من كل وجه فالعلمان متباينان على الإطلاق، وإن كان أعم منه، فالعلمان متداخلان وإن كان موضوعهما شيئاً واحداً بالذات متغايراً بالاعتبار أو شيئين متشاركين في جنس أو غيره، فالعلمان متناسبان على تفاصيل ذكرت في موضعها وبالجملة فقد أطبقوا على امتناع أن يكون شيء واحد موضوعاً لعلمين من غير اعتبار تغاير، بأن يؤخذ في أحدهما مطلقاً، وفي الآخر مقيداً أو يؤخذ في كل منهما مقيداً بقيد آخر، وامتناع أن يكون موضوع علم واحد شيئين من غير اعتبار اتحادهما في جنس أو غاية أو غيرهما، إذ لا معنى لاتحاد العلم واختلافه بدون.
I say: The consensus of the scholars is that the differentiation of the sciences in themselves is only according to the differentiation of their subjects. So it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject to benefit what distinguishes it in terms of essence, after the definition gave the distinction in terms of concept. Also, to know the aspect of unity for the multiplicity that one aims to encompass in summary, so that when one intends to obtain its details, the quest does not turn from what belongs to it to what does not. There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject, for in it is their commonality and by it is their unity, as we will detail. The verification of the matter is that when they attempted to know the states of things as much as human capacity allows, which is intended by wisdom, and they made the realities into types and genera and others, like human, animal, and existent, and they researched their specific states and proved them for them with evidence, so there resulted for them acquired propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities; they called them 'issues' (masa'il). And they made each group of them that goes back to one of those things—whether its subject is itself, a part of it, a type of it, or an essential accident of it—a particular science, distinguished by compilation, naming, and teaching, considering what that group, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, has of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then it may be unified from other aspects, like benefit, purpose, and the like. And for it there is taken from some of those aspects what benefits its conception in summary, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named object, i.e., that composite conceptual thing, as is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science of such-and-such rules'; otherwise it is a description (rasm), as is said: 'It is a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such' or 'by which one guards against such-and-such', or 'it is a tool for such-and-such'. Thus it became clear that the subject is the aspect of unity of the issues of a single science in terms of their essence, even if they have other aspects like definition and purpose. For there is no meaning to one thing being a science and another being a different science except that this one investigates the states of one thing and that one investigates the states of another thing different from it essentially or by consideration. So the differentiation of the sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, even if they are differentiated for the student through their definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the divergence (tabayun), proportionality (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject, meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is completely divergent from the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely divergent; if it is more general than it, then they are overlapping; and if their subject is one thing essentially but differs by consideration, or two things sharing in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportional, with details mentioned in their place. In summary, they have agreed on the impossibility of one thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as taking it absolute in one and restricted in the other, or taking it in each restricted by another restriction; and the impossibility of the subject of one science being two things without consideration of their unity in genus, purpose, or otherwise, for there is no meaning to the unity and difference of a science without that.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument by al-Taftazani explaining why the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues. He begins by stating the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects, so it is fitting to begin a science by stating its subject. The subject provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and also reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, preventing the student from straying into unrelated matters. He then verifies the matter by describing the process of scientific inquiry: scholars study the states of things (realities) and obtain propositions (issues) whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities. They then group these issues under a single subject (whether the subject itself, its part, its type, or its essential accident) and designate that group as a particular science. The unity of these issues under the subject is primary, though other unities (like benefit or purpose) may also be considered. The subject may be used to define the science (hadd) if it indicates the reality of the science, or to describe it (rasm) if it indicates the power or purpose. He concludes that the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the subject, even though students may differentiate them by definitions or purposes. This leads to the classification of sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional based on the relationship between their subjects. Finally, he notes the consensus that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu')
مسائل (masa'il)
تمييز (tamyiz)
جهة وحدة (jiha al-wahda)
حد (hadd)
رسم (rasm)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that sciences are distinguished merely by their definitions or purposes.
Correction: Al-Taftazani clarifies that the true distinction is by the subject (mawdu') in essence, while definitions and purposes are secondary and serve for the student's understanding.

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, what is the primary factor that distinguishes one science from another?
A) The definition of the science
B) The purpose (ghaya) of the science
C) The subject (mawdu') of the science
D) The benefit of the science

Key Takeaway
The subject is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues, even though definitions and purposes may also serve as secondary aspects.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you learn a new science, do you first ask 'What is its subject?' or do you just dive into its issues? How would starting with the subject change your understanding? Make a commitment to always ask 'What is the subject?' before studying any discipline, starting with your next lesson.
?
Your knowledge of deen—what is its subject? Is it merely about rules or about knowing Allah? Pause and write down: what single subject unifies everything you learn in your faith? If you can't name it, you may be collecting scattered facts without unity. Resolve to identify the subject of your religious learning today.
?
Have you ever confused the purpose of a science with its true subject? For example, treating kalam as merely a tool to debate rather than a science about the states of existence? Search your heart: have you studied kalam for intellectual pride or for knowing the truth? Repent and reorient your intention to seek the subject itself—the reality of things as they are.

How Subjects Determine Scientific Relationships
Al-Taftazani classifies scientific relationships based on subjects.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how al-Taftazani classifies the relationships between sciences (divergent, overlapping, proportional) based on their subjects, and the rules regarding one subject for two sciences and two subjects for one science.

Core Idea
The subject is the basis for classifying sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional.

After establishing that the subject is the primary factor for distinguishing sciences, al-Taftazani now applies this principle to explain the possible relationships between any two sciences. He introduces three categories: tabayun (divergence), tadakhul (overlap), and tanasub (proportionality), all defined by the relationship between their subjects. He also states two important impossibilities: one thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some distinction, and a single science cannot have two subjects without a unifying factor.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relationships
Read the second part starting from 'ولهذا جعلوا تباين العلوم...' and its translation. Focus on the three categories.
Notice the three terms: tabayun, tadakhul, tanasub.
2
Identify the three categories
For each category (divergent, overlapping, proportional), find the condition given in terms of subject relationships.
Divergent: subjects are completely different; Overlapping: one subject is more general; Proportional: subjects share a genus or are one thing under different considerations.
3
Understand the two impossibilities
He states two impossibilities: one thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some difference in consideration, and one science cannot have two subjects without a unifying factor.
These reinforce that the subject is the defining element.
4
Reflect on the overall principle
Consider how this classification shows that the subject is central to scientific identity and relationships.
The entire classification is built on the relationship between subjects.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

اتحادها على ما سنفصله، وتحقيق المقام أنهم لما حاولوا معرفة أحوال الأشياء بقدر الطاقة البشرية على ما هو المراد بالحكمة وصنعوا الحقائق أنواعاً وأجناساً وغيرها، كالإنسان والحيوان والموجود وبحثوا عن نوعاً منه أو عرضاً ذاتياً له علماً خاصاً يفرد بالتدوين والتسمية والتعليم نظراً إلى ما لتلك الطائفة على كثرتها، واختلاف محمولاتها من الاتحاد من جهة الموضوع أي الاشتراك فيه على الوجه المذكور ثم قد يتحد من جهات أخرى كالمنفعة والغاية ونحوهما، ويؤخذ لها من بعض تلك الجهات ما يفيد تصورها إجمالاً، ومن حيث إن لها وحدة فيكون حداً للعلم إن دل على حقيقة مسماه أعني ذلك المركب الاعتباري كما يقال: هو علم يبحث فيه عن كذا أو علم بقواعد كذا، وإلا فرسماً كما يقال هو علم يقتدر به على كذا أو يحترز عن كذا، أو يكون آلة لكذا، فظهر أن الموضوع هو جهة وحدة مسائل العلم الواحد نظراً إلى ذاتها وإن عرضت لها جهات أخر كالتعريف والغاية، فإنه لا معنى لكون هذا علماً وذاك علماً آخر سوى أنه يبحث هذا عن أحوال شيء وذلك عن أحوال شيء آخر مغاير له بالذات أو بالاعتبار، فلا يكون تمايز العلوم في أنفسها، وبالنظر إلى ذواتها إلا بحسب الموضوع، وإن كانت تتمايز عند الطالب بما لها من التعريفات والغايات ونحوهما، ولهذا جعلوا تباين العلوم وتناسبها وتداخلها أيضاً بحسب الموضوع، بمعنى أن موضوع أحد العلمين إن كان مبايناً لموضوع الآخر من كل وجه فالعلمان متباينان على الإطلاق، وإن كان أعم منه، فالعلمان متداخلان وإن كان موضوعهما شيئاً واحداً بالذات متغايراً بالاعتبار أو شيئين متشاركين في جنس أو غيره، فالعلمان متناسبان على تفاصيل ذكرت في موضعها وبالجملة فقد أطبقوا على امتناع أن يكون شيء واحد موضوعاً لعلمين من غير اعتبار تغاير، بأن يؤخذ في أحدهما مطلقاً، وفي الآخر مقيداً أو يؤخذ في كل منهما مقيداً بقيد آخر، وامتناع أن يكون موضوع علم واحد شيئين من غير اعتبار اتحادهما في جنس أو غاية أو غيرهما، إذ لا معنى لاتحاد العلم واختلافه بدون.
I say: The consensus of the scholars is that the differentiation of the sciences in themselves is only according to the differentiation of their subjects. So it is appropriate to begin a science by stating its subject to benefit what distinguishes it in terms of essence, after the definition gave the distinction in terms of concept. Also, to know the aspect of unity for the multiplicity that one aims to encompass in summary, so that when one intends to obtain its details, the quest does not turn from what belongs to it to what does not. There is no doubt that the aspect of unity of the issues of a science primarily and essentially is the subject, for in it is their commonality and by it is their unity, as we will detail. The verification of the matter is that when they attempted to know the states of things as much as human capacity allows, which is intended by wisdom, and they made the realities into types and genera and others, like human, animal, and existent, and they researched their specific states and proved them for them with evidence, so there resulted for them acquired propositions whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities; they called them 'issues' (masa'il). And they made each group of them that goes back to one of those things—whether its subject is itself, a part of it, a type of it, or an essential accident of it—a particular science, distinguished by compilation, naming, and teaching, considering what that group, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, has of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then it may be unified from other aspects, like benefit, purpose, and the like. And for it there is taken from some of those aspects what benefits its conception in summary, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named object, i.e., that composite conceptual thing, as is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science of such-and-such rules'; otherwise it is a description (rasm), as is said: 'It is a science by which one is empowered to do such-and-such' or 'by which one guards against such-and-such', or 'it is a tool for such-and-such'. Thus it became clear that the subject is the aspect of unity of the issues of a single science in terms of their essence, even if they have other aspects like definition and purpose. For there is no meaning to one thing being a science and another being a different science except that this one investigates the states of one thing and that one investigates the states of another thing different from it essentially or by consideration. So the differentiation of the sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, even if they are differentiated for the student through their definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the divergence (tabayun), proportionality (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject, meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is completely divergent from the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely divergent; if it is more general than it, then they are overlapping; and if their subject is one thing essentially but differs by consideration, or two things sharing in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportional, with details mentioned in their place. In summary, they have agreed on the impossibility of one thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as taking it absolute in one and restricted in the other, or taking it in each restricted by another restriction; and the impossibility of the subject of one science being two things without consideration of their unity in genus, purpose, or otherwise, for there is no meaning to the unity and difference of a science without that.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage is an extended argument by al-Taftazani explaining why the subject (mawdu') is the essential factor for distinguishing sciences and providing unity to their issues. He begins by stating the consensus that sciences are differentiated by their subjects, so it is fitting to begin a science by stating its subject. The subject provides both conceptual and essential distinction, and also reveals the aspect of unity among the diverse issues of that science, preventing the student from straying into unrelated matters. He then verifies the matter by describing the process of scientific inquiry: scholars study the states of things (realities) and obtain propositions (issues) whose predicates are essential accidents of those realities. They then group these issues under a single subject (whether the subject itself, its part, its type, or its essential accident) and designate that group as a particular science. The unity of these issues under the subject is primary, though other unities (like benefit or purpose) may also be considered. The subject may be used to define the science (hadd) if it indicates the reality of the science, or to describe it (rasm) if it indicates the power or purpose. He concludes that the differentiation of sciences in themselves is only by the subject, even though students may differentiate them by definitions or purposes. This leads to the classification of sciences as divergent, overlapping, or proportional based on the relationship between their subjects. Finally, he notes the consensus that a single thing cannot be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

Key Terms
تباين (tabayun)
تداخل (tadakhul)
تناسب (tanasub)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: One might think that two sciences can share the exact same subject without any difference.
Correction: Al-Taftazani states it is impossible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences without some consideration of difference (e.g., absolute vs. restricted).

Checkpoint
According to al-Taftazani, when are two sciences considered overlapping (mutaqadakhilan)?
A) When their subjects are completely different
B) When the subject of one is more general than the subject of the other
C) When their subjects are identical
D) When they share a common genus

Key Takeaway
The relationships between sciences are determined by the relationships between their subjects; a single subject cannot belong to two sciences without some distinction, and a single science cannot have two subjects without some unifying consideration.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own studies of Islamic knowledge, do you ever treat different subjects (like tafsir and fiqh) as if they are completely separate, or do you see them as overlapping or proportional? How does al-Taftazani's classification challenge you to reconsider the unity of knowledge you seek?
?
Consider the subject of your life's pursuit: is it singular or multiple? If multiple, what unifying purpose ties them together? This lesson warns against a science having two unrelated subjects—what does this say about your own intellectual focus? Are you wasting effort by not identifying the true subject of your learning?
?
When you approach a new topic, do you first ask 'What is its subject?' and how it relates to other things you know? If not, what confusion might result? Commit to starting every study session by clarifying the subject and its relation to other sciences.

Subject Matter Unifies a Science
Definition of a science and the primacy of its subject matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the subject matter (mawdu') is the essential criterion for the unity and differentiation of the sciences, and how definitions of a science can vary but still point to the same subject.

Core Idea
The subject is the glue that holds a science together.

This lesson introduces a foundational principle in Islamic epistemology: that a science is defined and distinguished not by its goals or predicates, but by its subject matter. The author explains how even though a science may be defined in multiple ways—by its purpose, its rules, or its function—what truly makes it one science is the single subject it investigates. This concept will become the basis for understanding how sciences relate to each other.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on definitions
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Notice how the author discusses different ways to define a science.
2
Identify the key claim
Find the sentence where the author states that the differentiation of sciences is only according to the subject.
It is near the end of the passage.
3
Understand the role of definitions
The author distinguishes between a true definition (hadd) and a description (rasm). Note how they relate to the subject.
A hadd indicates the reality of the science, a rasm indicates its function.
4
Reflect on the implication
Why is the subject the only essential factor? Consider what would happen if we used other factors like purpose.
Purpose can vary while the subject remains the same.

By codification, naming, and instruction, considering what that group has, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then, it may be united from other aspects (1) like benefit, purpose, and the like, and from some of those aspects something is taken that gives an overall conception of it, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named thing (2), namely that composite conceptual entity, as it is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science about such-and-such rules', otherwise it is a description (rasm) (3) as it is said: 'It is a science by which such-and-such is accomplished' or 'by which such-and-such is guarded against', or 'it is an instrument for such-and-such'. So it becomes clear that the subject (mawdu') is the aspect of unity of the problems of a single science in respect of their essences, even if other aspects like definition and purpose present themselves. For there is no meaning (4) to this being one science and that being another science except that this one investigates the states of something and that one investigates the states of something else different from it in essence or by consideration. So the differentiation of sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, although they are differentiated for the student by what they have of definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the contrast (tabayun), proportion (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject: meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is entirely juxtaposed to the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely contrasted; if it is more general than it, then the two sciences overlap; and if their subject is one thing in essence but different by consideration, or two things that share in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportionate, with details mentioned in their place (6). In sum, they have agreed on the impossibility of a single thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as being taken in one absolutely and in the other restrictively, or being taken in each restrictively with a different restriction; and the impossibility of a single science having two things as its subject without consideration of their unity in genus or purpose or otherwise, since there is no meaning to the unity or difference of a science without that. It cannot be said: 'Science differs (1) by the difference of the known, i.e., the problems, and they differ by the difference of the subject, so why not also differ (3) by the difference of the predicate? Why not make this the basis of differentiation, such that investigating some of the essential attributes (a'rad dhatiyya) is one science, and investigating some others is another science while the subject is united, especially since this is closer based on the subject being like matter (madda) which is the source of the genus, and the essential attributes being like form (sura), the source of the differentia (fasl) which perfects distinction?' Our answer then is: The matter of unity and difference cannot be regulated, and every science would become many sciences necessarily due to its containing many types of essential attributes. For example, arithmetic would become many sciences due to the multiplicity of the predicates of its problems, such as even, odd, even-even, even-odd, and so on; likewise all sciences. The error arises only from failing to distinguish between 'science' in the sense of a discipline (sina'a), i.e., all the investigations related to a certain subject, and 'science' in the sense of the attainment of the form. If the latter were intended, then every problem would be a science by itself. Also, the basis of unity and difference, and what follows from it of contrast, proportion, and overlap, must be something definite and clear or based on something, and that is the subject (4), for essential attributes cannot be restricted nor enumerated; rather one would be able to prove whatever he could, and they become evident only by their realization in the science itself. For this reason their definitions at the beginning of the science are nominal definitions, and perhaps (5) after proving them they become real definitions, unlike the definitions of the subject and its parts, for they are real. As for the talk of matter and form, it is false, because both the subject (6) and the predicate are a material part of the proposition; the formal is the
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage argues that the subject matter (mawdu') is the sole criterion for the unity and differentiation of the sciences (al-‘ulūm). The author begins by noting that a science may be defined in various ways—by its subject, its purpose, or its function—but what truly makes a science one science is its subject. He explains that two sciences are distinguished only by investigating different subjects, not by having different predicates or goals. He then discusses the relationships between sciences: if two sciences have completely separate subjects, they are entirely distinct (mutabāyinān); if one subject is more general, they overlap (mutadākhilān); if they share a genus or are the same in essence but considered differently, they are proportionate (mutanāsibān). He asserts that scholars agree it is impossible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences without some difference in how it is considered, and conversely, for one science to have two different subjects without some unifying aspect. The author then addresses a potential objection: why not differentiate sciences by their predicates (al-maḥmūlāt) or essential attributes (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya), making each set of attributes a separate science? He refutes this by arguing that such a criterion would lead to an uncontrolled proliferation of sciences, as every science contains numerous predicates. For instance, arithmetic would splinter into countless sciences based on properties like evenness and oddness. The error, he says, comes from confusing 'science' as a discipline (al-ṣinā‘a)—the entire body of inquiry about a subject—with 'science' as the acquisition of a single form (ṣūra). The subject is the only stable basis for defining and distinguishing sciences, especially since essential attributes cannot be exhaustively listed before the science is developed. He concludes by dismissing the analogy that subject is like matter and attributes like form, stating that both subject and predicate are material parts of a proposition, not form and matter.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawdu')
علم (ʿilm)
حد (ḥadd)
رسم (rasm)
محمول (maḥmūl)

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the essential basis for distinguishing one science from another?
A) Its definitions (ḥudūd)
B) Its subject matter (mawḍūʿ)
C) Its predicates (maḥmūlāt)
D) Its benefits (manāfiʿ)

Key Takeaway
A science is defined and distinguished from other sciences by its subject matter, not by its definitions or purposes.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You have just learned that a science's true identity lies in its subject, not in how it is defined or described. In your own journey of seeking Islamic knowledge, have you ever focused more on memorizing definitions or goals rather than grasping the core subject of each science? How can you change your approach to prioritize the subject starting from your next lesson?
?
The passage states that the subject unifies the problems of a science. Consider your own life: what is the subject that unifies your actions and pursuits? Are you allowing secondary benefits or purposes to define your path, or are you anchored to the essence? Take a moment to identify and commit to the core subject of your life's aim.
?
If the differentiation of sciences is by subject, then what about the science of knowing Allah? Have you been studying Kalam, Tafsir, or Fiqh merely for academic purposes, or have you kept your heart focused on the subject—the Creator Himself? Pray that your study of these sciences leads you to true knowledge of Allah, not just information.

How Sciences Relate: Contrast, Proportion, and Overlap
Classification of relationships between sciences based on subject matter.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the relationships between sciences are determined by their subject matters, including concepts of contrast (tabāyun), proportion (tanāsub), and overlap (tadākhul).

Core Idea
Sciences relate exactly as their subjects relate.

Having established that the subject matter is the core of a science, the author now explains how sciences relate to each other. If their subjects are entirely different, the sciences are unrelated; if one subject is broader, the sciences overlap; if they share a genus or are the same in essence but considered differently, they are proportionate. These relationships are essential for understanding the organization of knowledge.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage on relationships
Read the Arabic and translation. Focus on the three types of relationships.
2
Define each relationship
For each of tabāyun, tadākhul, tanāsub, state the condition based on subject.
Tabāyun: subjects entirely different; Tadākhul: one subject more general; Tanāsub: subjects share genus or same in essence but different consideration.
3
Understand the impossibilities
Note the two impossibilities: one subject for two sciences without difference, and one science with two subjects without unity.
This underscores the necessity of the subject as the basis.
4
Apply to examples
Think of pairs of sciences (e.g., physics and chemistry) and consider their subject relationship.
This is speculative; the passage does not give examples.

متباينان على الإطلاق، وإن كان أعم منه، فالعلمان متداخلان وإن كان موضوعهما شيئاً واحداً بالذات متغايراً بالاعتبار أو شيئين متشاركين في جنس أو غيره، فالعلمان متناسبان على تفاصيل ذكرت في موضعها (٦) وبالجملة فقد أطبقوا على امتناع أن يكون شيء واحد موضوعاً لعلمين من غير اعتبار تغاير، بأن يؤخذ في أحدهما مطلقاً، وفي الآخر مقيداً أو يؤخذ في كل منهما مقيداً بقيد آخر، وامتناع أن يكون هو (۷) موضوع علم By codification, naming, and instruction, considering what that group has, despite its multiplicity and the difference of its predicates, of unity from the aspect of the subject, i.e., commonality in it in the mentioned manner. Then, it may be united from other aspects (1) like benefit, purpose, and the like, and from some of those aspects something is taken that gives an overall conception of it, and insofar as it has unity, it becomes a definition (hadd) of the science if it indicates the reality of its named thing (2), namely that composite conceptual entity, as it is said: 'It is a science in which such-and-such is investigated' or 'a science about such-and-such rules', otherwise it is a description (rasm) (3) as it is said: 'It is a science by which such-and-such is accomplished' or 'by which such-and-such is guarded against', or 'it is an instrument for such-and-such'. So it becomes clear that the subject (mawdu') is the aspect of unity of the problems of a single science in respect of their essences, even if other aspects like definition and purpose present themselves. For there is no meaning (4) to this being one science and that being another science except that this one investigates the states of something and that one investigates the states of something else different from it in essence or by consideration. So the differentiation of sciences in themselves and with respect to their essences is only according to the subject, although they are differentiated for the student by what they have of definitions, purposes, and the like. For this reason they made the contrast (tabayun), proportion (tanasub), and overlap (tadakhul) of sciences also according to the subject: meaning that if the subject of one of two sciences is entirely juxtaposed to the subject of the other, then the two sciences are absolutely contrasted; if it is more general than it, then the two sciences overlap; and if their subject is one thing in essence but different by consideration, or two things that share in a genus or otherwise, then the two sciences are proportionate, with details mentioned in their place (6). In sum, they have agreed on the impossibility of a single thing being the subject of two sciences without consideration of difference, such as being taken in one absolutely and in the other restrictively, or being taken in each restrictively with a different restriction; and the impossibility of a single science having two things as its subject without consideration of their unity in genus or purpose or otherwise, since there is no meaning to the unity or difference of a science without that. It cannot be said: 'Science differs (1) by the difference of the known, i.e., the problems, and they differ by the difference of the subject, so why not also differ (3) by the difference of the predicate? Why not make this the basis of differentiation, such that investigating some of the essential attributes (a'rad dhatiyya) is one science, and investigating some others is another science while the subject is united, especially since this is closer based on the subject being like matter (madda) which is the source of the genus, and the essential attributes being like form (sura), the source of the differentia (fasl) which perfects distinction?' Our answer then is: The matter of unity and difference cannot be regulated, and every science would become many sciences necessarily due to its containing many types of essential attributes. For example, arithmetic would become many sciences due to the multiplicity of the predicates of its problems, such as even, odd, even-even, even-odd, and so on; likewise all sciences. The error arises only from failing to distinguish between 'science' in the sense of a discipline (sina'a), i.e., all the investigations related to a certain subject, and 'science' in the sense of the attainment of the form. If the latter were intended, then every problem would be a science by itself. Also, the basis of unity and difference, and what follows from it of contrast, proportion, and overlap, must be something definite and clear or based on something, and that is the subject (4), for essential attributes cannot be restricted nor enumerated; rather one would be able to prove whatever he could, and they become evident only by their realization in the science itself. For this reason their definitions at the beginning of the science are nominal definitions, and perhaps (5) after proving them they become real definitions, unlike the definitions of the subject and its parts, for they are real. As for the talk of matter and form, it is false, because both the subject (6) and the predicate are a material part of the proposition; the formal is the
Source pages p. 168

Commentary
This passage argues that the subject matter (mawdu') is the sole criterion for the unity and differentiation of the sciences (al-‘ulūm). The author begins by noting that a science may be defined in various ways—by its subject, its purpose, or its function—but what truly makes a science one science is its subject. He explains that two sciences are distinguished only by investigating different subjects, not by having different predicates or goals. He then discusses the relationships between sciences: if two sciences have completely separate subjects, they are entirely distinct (mutabāyinān); if one subject is more general, they overlap (mutadākhilān); if they share a genus or are the same in essence but considered differently, they are proportionate (mutanāsibān). He asserts that scholars agree it is impossible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences without some difference in how it is considered, and conversely, for one science to have two different subjects without some unifying aspect. The author then addresses a potential objection: why not differentiate sciences by their predicates (al-maḥmūlāt) or essential attributes (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya), making each set of attributes a separate science? He refutes this by arguing that such a criterion would lead to an uncontrolled proliferation of sciences, as every science contains numerous predicates. For instance, arithmetic would splinter into countless sciences based on properties like evenness and oddness. The error, he says, comes from confusing 'science' as a discipline (al-ṣinā‘a)—the entire body of inquiry about a subject—with 'science' as the acquisition of a single form (ṣūra). The subject is the only stable basis for defining and distinguishing sciences, especially since essential attributes cannot be exhaustively listed before the science is developed. He concludes by dismissing the analogy that subject is like matter and attributes like form, stating that both subject and predicate are material parts of a proposition, not form and matter.

Key Terms
موضوع (mawḍūʿ)
تباين (tabāyun)
تناسب (tanāsub)
تداخل (tadākhul)

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the relationship between two sciences if one subject is more general than the other?
A) Tabāyun (contrast)
B) Tadākhul (overlap)
C) Tanāsub (proportion)
D) No relationship

Key Takeaway
The relationship between any two sciences is determined by the relationship between their subject matters.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In your own studies, do you tend to treat each subject as completely separate (tabayun), or do you see connections (tanasub/tadakhul)? How might an imbalanced approach distort your understanding of the truth? Will you commit to actively seeking the relationships between the different sciences you learn?
?
The passage teaches that one science cannot have two unrelated subjects. Is your pursuit of knowledge unified by a single purpose (like seeking Allah's pleasure), or are you fragmented across multiple goals? What would it take to bring your studies under one guiding aim?
?
If two sciences cannot study the same thing without a difference in consideration, how carefully do you examine the 'angle' from which you approach each subject? Have you ever confused a theological question with a philosophical one by ignoring their different considerations? How can you guard against this in your learning?

Method of Learning

Why Predicates Cannot Differentiate Sciences
Objection and refutation regarding differentiation by predicates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the objection that sciences could be differentiated by their predicates or essential attributes, and the author's refutation showing why this leads to chaos.

Core Idea
Predicates are too many to define sciences; only the subject brings order.

After establishing that the subject is the basis, the author anticipates an objection: why not differentiate sciences by their predicates or essential attributes, since they are what the science actually investigates? He then refutes this by showing that such a criterion would lead to an infinite multiplication of sciences, using arithmetic as an example. He also clarifies the distinction between 'science' as a discipline and 'science' as the acquisition of a single form.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection
Read the objection carefully. Understand the proposal to use predicates and the matter-form analogy.
2
Read the refutation
Follow the author's three arguments: infinite multiplication, distinction of 'science', and the unregulatable nature of attributes.
3
Analyze the arithmetic example
See how arithmetic would splinter into sciences of even, odd, etc.
This illustrates the practical problem.
4
Grasp the key distinction
Understand the difference between science as discipline (ṣināʿa) and science as form (ṣūra).
Discipline includes all problems on a subject; form would be each problem.
5
Evaluate the analogy rejection
The author denies that subject is matter and attributes are form, because both are material parts of a proposition.
The formal aspect is not elaborated here.

Commentary
This passage argues that the subject matter (mawdu') is the sole criterion for the unity and differentiation of the sciences (al-‘ulūm). The author begins by noting that a science may be defined in various ways—by its subject, its purpose, or its function—but what truly makes a science one science is its subject. He explains that two sciences are distinguished only by investigating different subjects, not by having different predicates or goals. He then discusses the relationships between sciences: if two sciences have completely separate subjects, they are entirely distinct (mutabāyinān); if one subject is more general, they overlap (mutadākhilān); if they share a genus or are the same in essence but considered differently, they are proportionate (mutanāsibān). He asserts that scholars agree it is impossible for one thing to be the subject of two sciences without some difference in how it is considered, and conversely, for one science to have two different subjects without some unifying aspect. The author then addresses a potential objection: why not differentiate sciences by their predicates (al-maḥmūlāt) or essential attributes (al-a‘rāḍ al-dhātiyya), making each set of attributes a separate science? He refutes this by arguing that such a criterion would lead to an uncontrolled proliferation of sciences, as every science contains numerous predicates. For instance, arithmetic would splinter into countless sciences based on properties like evenness and oddness. The error, he says, comes from confusing 'science' as a discipline (al-ṣinā‘a)—the entire body of inquiry about a subject—with 'science' as the acquisition of a single form (ṣūra). The subject is the only stable basis for defining and distinguishing sciences, especially since essential attributes cannot be exhaustively listed before the science is developed. He concludes by dismissing the analogy that subject is like matter and attributes like form, stating that both subject and predicate are material parts of a proposition, not form and matter.

صورة (ṣūra)

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sciences can be distinguished by their predicates or essential attributes.
Correction: The author argues that predicates are too numerous and cannot regulate unity and difference; only the subject provides a stable basis.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what would happen if we tried to differentiate sciences by their predicates?
A) It would be more precise.
B) It would lead to an infinite number of sciences.
C) It would unify sciences.
D) It would be impossible to do.

Key Takeaway
Differentiating sciences by their predicates is unworkable because predicates are too numerous; the subject is the only stable and definite basis.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you approach a new field of study, do you naturally focus on the various results and specific problems (like predicates) rather than the core subject? How does that tendency fragment your understanding? What would change if you prioritized the subject first?
?
The author warns that confusing science as a discipline with science as a form makes every problem its own science. In your learning, have you ever treated each problem in isolation, losing sight of the unified subject? What was the cost, and how can you now keep the subject central?
?
The objector thought that the 'form' (predicates) gives definition. In your life, do you let external markers of success (like achievements or knowledge) define who you are, rather than the deeper 'subject' of your purpose? How can you realign your identity to what truly matters?

Why Sciences Cannot Be Differentiated by Accidental Attributes
Refutation of differentiating sciences by essential accidents; defense of subject matter as basis.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Islamic scholars reject differentiating sciences based on accidental attributes, and instead insist on the subject matter as the foundational criterion.

Core Idea
Essential accidents are boundless; the subject matter alone provides stable differentiation.

This passage confronts a tempting proposal: if sciences differ by their problems and predicates, why not classify them by those predicates? The author argues that such an approach would lead to endless fragmentation. Instead, he restates the classical position that the subject matter (al-mawḍūʿ) is the only reliable basis for unifying a science.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Notice the argument's flow: proposal, refutation, example, and conclusion.
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the proposal being refuted
The author presents a hypothetical objection: why not differentiate sciences by the essential accidents investigated, even if the subject matter is the same? Find this in the text.
This proposal seems plausible because essential accidents are like form (differentiating).
3
Understand the refutation
The author argues that this method leads to chaos: essential accidents are countless and uncontrollable, so every science would split into countless sub-sciences, as shown with arithmetic.
The example of arithmetic illustrates the absurdity.
4
Grasp the distinction between two meanings of 'science'
The author points out the error of confusing 'science as a craft' (sina'a) with 'science as acquisition of a form' (husul al-sura). The former encompasses all investigations of a subject; the latter would make each problem a separate science.
This distinction is key to the argument.
5
Learn why the subject matter is the proper basis
The author concludes that unity and difference must rest on a determinate basis—the subject matter—because essential accidents cannot be controlled or enumerated. Moreover, definitions of the subject matter are real, while those of essential accidents are initially only nominal.
This solidifies the classical criterion for distinguishing sciences.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وهي كما ذلك، لا يقال العلم مختلف باختلاف المعلوم أعني المسائل، وهي تختلف باختلاف الموضوع، فكذا تختلف باختلاف المحمول، فلم لم يجعل هذا وجه التمايز بأن يكون البحث عن بعض من الأعراض الذاتية علماً، وعن بعض آخر علماً آخر مع اتحاد الموضوع، على أن هذا أقرب بناء على كون الموضوع بمنزلة المادة وهي مأخذ للجنس، والأعراض الذاتية بمنزلة الصورة، مأخذ للفصل الذي به كمال التميز : لأنا نقول حينئذ : لا ينضبط أمر الاتحاد والاختلاف ويكون كل علم علوماً جمة ضرورة اشتماله على أنواع جمة من الأعراض الذاتية مثلا يكون الحساب علوماً متعددة بتعدد محمولات المسائل من الزوج والفرد وزوج الزوج وزوج الفرد إلى غير ذلك، وكذا سائر العلوم، والغلط إنما نشأ من عدم التفرقة بين العلم بمعنى الصناعة أعني جميع المباحث المتعلقة بموضوع ما . وبين العلم بمعنى حصول الصورة ولو أريد هذا لكان كل مسألة علماً على حدة، وأيضاً مبنى الاتحاد والاختلاف، وما يتبعه من التباين والتناسب والتداخل يجب أن يكون أمراً معيناً بيناً أو مبنياً وذلك هو الموضوع إذ لا ضبط للأعراض الذاتية ولا حصر ، بل لكان أحد أن يثبت ما استطاع، وإنما يتبين بتحققها في العلم نفسه، ولهذا كانت حدودها في صدر العلم حدوداً اسمية، وربما تصير بعد إثباتها حدوداً حقيقية بخلاف حدود الموضوع وأجزائه، فإنها حقيقية
And it is like that. It is not said that knowledge differs by the difference of the known, meaning the problems, and they differ by difference of the subject matter, so also they differ by difference of the predicate. So why was this not made the aspect of differentiation, such that the investigation of some of the essential accidents becomes a science, and of some others another science, while the subject matter is unified, especially since this is closer based on the subject being in the position of matter and it is the source of the genus, and the essential accidents are in the position of form, the source of the specific difference by which complete differentiation occurs? For we say then: The matter of unity and difference is not controlled, and every science would become numerous sciences due to the necessity of its containing numerous types of essential accidents. For example, arithmetic would become multiple sciences by the multiplicity of the predicates of its problems, such as even, odd, even-even, even-odd, and so on, and likewise the rest of the sciences. The error only arose from the failure to distinguish between knowledge in the sense of the craft, I mean all the investigations related to a subject matter, and between knowledge in the sense of acquisition of the form. If this meaning were intended, then every problem would be a science on its own. Also, the basis of unity and difference, and what follows from it of contrast, proportion, and overlap, must be a determinate matter, evident or founded, and that is the subject matter, because the essential accidents have no control and no limit; rather, one could prove whatever he is able, and it only becomes evident by their verification in the science itself. For this reason, their definitions in the beginning of the science are nominal definitions, and perhaps after their proof they become real definitions, unlike the definitions of the subject matter and its parts, for they are real.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage refutes the proposal that sciences should be differentiated by their essential accidents (al-a'rad al-dhatiyya) instead of the subject matter (al-mawdu'). The author presents the proposal as a possible objection: why not make the investigation of some essential accidents a distinct science even if the subject matter is the same? The argument for this proposal is that since the subject matter is like matter (the genus), and essential accidents are like form (the specific difference), they seem to provide perfect differentiation. However, the author immediately refutes this by pointing out that such a method would lead to chaos: essential accidents are countless and uncontrollable, so each science would split into countless sub-sciences. The example of arithmetic is given: predicates like 'even', 'odd', 'even-even', etc., would each require a separate science. The root error is failing to distinguish between 'science' as a discipline (sina'a) comprising all investigations related to a subject, and 'science' as the mere acquisition of a form (husul al-sura), which would make each single problem a science. The author concludes that the unification and differentiation of sciences must rest on a determinate basis—the subject matter—since essential accidents cannot be controlled or enumerated. Moreover, definitions of essential accidents are only nominal at the outset and become real only after proof, whereas definitions of the subject matter and its parts are real from the start.

Checkpoint
Why does the author reject the idea of differentiating sciences by essential accidents?
A) Because essential accidents are too numerous and cannot be controlled.
B) Because essential accidents are not real attributes.
C) Because the subject matter is easier to define.
D) Because essential accidents change over time.

Key Takeaway
The differentiation of sciences rests on a determinate basis—the subject matter—not on the countless essential accidents, which would lead to fragmentation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you treat the boundaries of your own knowledge (e.g., fields you study) as based on subject matter or on accidental features like the teacher, the book, or the ease of topics? Examine honestly: when you pick up a new book or course, what determines whether you consider it 'part of the same science' or 'different'? How does that affect your learning?
?
The author warns that taking each proposition as its own science leads to fragmentation. Where in your life have you fragmented knowledge into disconnected pieces, ignoring the unifying subject? What would change if you reorganized your understanding around core subjects rather than scattered facts?
?
You claim to seek truth, but do you actually welcome the discipline of a unified subject, or do you prefer picking and choosing accidental fragments that suit your whim? This lesson demands you choose: are you willing to submit to the subject's determinacy, or will you remain lost in the endless diversity of accidental attributes? Commit to one way today.

The Aspect of Investigation Unifies a Science
The author refutes matter-form analogy, presents objection, and answers with aspect of investigation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the unity of a science is determined by the aspect (jiha) of investigation, not merely by a shared subject genus.

Core Idea
The aspect of investigation, not the subject matter, makes a science one.

In this passage, the author defends the view that sciences are unified by their formal aspect—the perspective from which they examine things—rather than by their material subject. He refutes the matter-form analogy, addresses an objection about arithmetic and geometry sharing quantity, and clarifies how the aspect of investigation resolves the issue. Let's read his argument carefully.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and its translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then the translation. Pay attention to the objection (فإن قلت) and the answer (قلت).
The passage is dense; take your time.
2
Identify the two positions
Notice how the author first rejects the matter-form analogy, then defines science as a composite entity where problems are matter and the aspect is form. Then he presents an objection: arithmetic and geometry share the genus of quantity but are separate sciences. His answer relies on the aspect of investigation.
This objection is key.
3
Apply the concept to grammar
Consider the example of grammar. The author contrasts grammar with arithmetic/geometry to illustrate how the aspect of investigation differs. Reflect on how different sciences can study the same category (like quantity) but from different aspects, making them distinct.
This connects to the learning objective.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما حديث المادة والصورة فكاذب، لأن كلاً من الموضوع والمحمول جزء مادي من القضية، وإنما الصوري هو الحكم على أن الكلام ليس في المسألة بل في المركب الاعتباري الذي هو العلم ولا خفاء في أن المسائل مادة له ومرجع الصورة إلى جهة الاتحاد إذ بها تصير المسائل تلك الصناعة المخصوصة فإن قلت: اشتراط تشارك موضوعات العلم الواحد في جنس أو في غيره لا يدفع اختلال أمر اتحاد العلم واختلافه إذ قلما يخلو موضوعا العلمين عن تشارك في ذاتي أو عرضي أقله الوجود بل مثل الحساب والهندسة الباحثين عن العدد والمقدار الداخلين تحت جنس هو الكم لا يجعل علماً واحداً بل علمين متساويين في الرتبة، بخلاف علم النحو الباحث عن أنواع الكلمة قلت: إذا كان البحث عن الأشياء من جهة اشتراكها في ذلك الأمر ومصداقه أن يقع البحث عن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك فالعلم واحد وإلا فمتعدد، ألا ترى أن الحساب والهندسة لا ينظران في الزمان الذي هو من أنواع الكم، وإلى هذا يشير كلام الشفاء أن كلاً من الحساب والهندسة إنما يجعل علماً على حدة
As for the talk about matter and form, it is false, because each of the subject and the predicate is a material part of the proposition; rather, the formal is the judgment, upon the consideration that the discussion is not about the problem but about the composite entity which is the science. And there is no hiding that the problems are matter for it, and the reference of the form is to the aspect of unity, because through it the problems become that particular craft. If you say: The condition of the subject matters of a single science sharing a genus or something else does not repel the confusion of the matter of the unity and difference of sciences, since the subject matters of two sciences rarely lack sharing in an essential or accidental attribute, the least of which is existence. Rather, like arithmetic and geometry, which investigate number and magnitude, both falling under the genus of quantity, this does not make them a single science but two sciences equal in rank, unlike the science of grammar which investigates the types of the word. I say: When the investigation is about things from the aspect of their sharing in that matter, and its verification is that the investigation covers everything that shares with them in that, then the science is one; otherwise it is multiple. Do you not see that arithmetic and geometry do not consider time, which is among the types of quantity? And to this the speech of al-Shifa points, that each of arithmetic and geometry is made a science on its own.
Source pages p. 169

Commentary
This passage begins by rejecting the analogy that subject matter is matter and essential accidents are form in differentiating sciences. The author states that both subject and predicate are material parts of a proposition; the formal aspect is the judgment itself. The discussion is not about individual problems but about the composite entity called a science, where problems serve as matter and the form is the aspect (jiha) of unity that makes them a specific discipline. Then an objection (فإن قلت) is raised: requiring that subject matters of a single science share a genus does not solve the problem because even sciences like arithmetic and geometry share in the genus of quantity (al-kamm), yet they are separate sciences. Also, grammar investigates types of words. The answer (قلت) clarifies that unity is determined by the aspect of investigation: if the investigation considers things from the aspect of their sharing in a certain property, and truly covers everything that shares that property, then it is one science; otherwise multiple. Arithmetic and geometry both study quantity but from different aspects—arithmetic studies discrete quantity (number) and geometry studies continuous quantity (magnitude)—and neither considers time (another type of quantity). Thus, the aspect of investigation (jiha) is the formal cause of unity, not mere commonality of subject matter. The author references Ibn Sina's al-Shifa to support this.

المركب الاعتباري
جهة الاتحاد
الكم
الحساب
الهندسة
علم النحو
كلام الشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think that if two subject matters share a genus (e.g., quantity), the sciences investigating them must be one.
Correction: The author clarifies that the aspect of investigation (jiha) determines unity; arithmetic and geometry both study quantity but from different aspects, making them separate sciences.

Checkpoint
According to the author, what determines whether a science is one or multiple?
A) The number of subject matters.
B) The genus under which the subject falls.
C) The aspect (jiha) of investigation.
D) The predicates used in its problems.

Key Takeaway
The aspect (jiha) of investigation is the formal principle that unifies a science, not the material subject matter or shared genus.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you study a subject like fiqh or tafsir, what is the aspect of investigation that unifies your learning? Is it the rulings themselves or the underlying principle of seeking Allah's guidance? How does recognizing that aspect change the way you approach each mas'ala?
?
The author says that sharing a genus does not make two investigations one science. Reflect on your own fields of knowledge. Are there areas you have mistakenly considered unified because they share a broad topic? How can you identify the true aspect of investigation that should guide your understanding?
?
The passage rejects the matter-form analogy for propositions and sciences. In your own thinking, where do you confuse the material parts (subject, predicate) with the formal element (judgment)? How can you ensure your reasoning is truly formal (valid) rather than material?

The Condition for the Unity of a Science
Sanusi argues the condition for unity of science and answers an objection using arithmetic and geometry.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand that the unity of a science depends on the perspective of investigation, not merely on shared subject matter or genus.

Core Idea
Unity of science is about the lens, not the object.

What makes a single science one? Is it enough that the subjects share a genus? Sanusi tackles this foundational question by clarifying that science is a composite mental entity, and its unity comes from the form or aspect under which its subject matters are examined. He anticipates an objection about arithmetic and geometry and provides a crisp answer that distinguishes between sharing a genus and sharing a perspective.

Guided Path
1
Clarify the object of discourse
Read the opening lines where Sanusi distinguishes discourse about the subject matter vs. the composite mental entity that is the science itself.
Notice that science is an organized body of knowledge, not just a collection of topics.
2
Understand the condition of unity
Identify the condition: sharing of subjects in a genus or something else, but then the objection arises that this does not guarantee unity.
The example of arithmetic and geometry illustrates the objection.
3
Grasp the resolution
See how Sanusi resolves that unity depends on investigating from the aspect of commonality, and that the absence of such a common perspective leads to multiple sciences.
He cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa for support.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الحكم على أن الكلام ليس في المسألة بل في المركب الاعتباري الذي هو العلم ولا خفاء في أن المسائل مادة له ومرجع الصورة إلى جهة الاتحاد إذ بها تصير المسائل تلك الصناعة المخصوصة فإن قلت: اشتراط تشارك موضوعات العلم الواحد في جنس أو في غيره لا يدفع اختلال أمر اتحاد العلم واختلافه إذ قلما يخلو موضوعا العلمين عن تشارك في ذاتي أو عرضي أقله الوجود بل مثل الحساب والهندسة الباحثين عن العدد والمقدار الداخلين تحت جنس هو الكم لا يجعل علماً واحداً بل علمين متساويين في الرتبة، بخلاف علم النحو الباحث عن أنواع الكلمة قلت: إذا كان البحث عن الأشياء من جهة اشتراكها في ذلك الأمر ومصداقه أن يقع البحث عن كل ما يشاركها في ذلك فالعلم واحد وإلا فمتعدد، ألا ترى أن الحساب والهندسة لا ينظران في الزمان الذي هو من أنواع الكم، وإلى هذا يشير كلام الشفاء أن كلاً من الحساب والهندسة إنما يجعل علماً على حدة فيسمى تالياً.
The judgment is that discourse is not about the subject matter but about the composite mental entity which is the science, and there is no hiddenness in that the subject matters are material for it. The form refers to the aspect of unity, for through it the subject matters become that particular discipline. If you say: The condition of sharing of the subjects of a single science in a genus or in something else does not ward off the confusion of the unity and difference of science, for rarely do the subjects of two sciences lack sharing in an essential or accidental thing, at least existence; rather like arithmetic and geometry, which investigate number and magnitude, which fall under a genus that is quantity, do not make one science but two sciences equal in rank, unlike the science of grammar, which investigates types of words. I say: If the investigation of things is from the aspect of their sharing in that matter and its verification is that the investigation occurs concerning everything that shares in that, then the science is one; otherwise it is multiple. Do you not see that arithmetic and geometry do not consider time, which is among the types of quantity? And to this points the speech of al-Shifa that each of arithmetic and geometry is made a science separately, so it is called consequent.
Source pages p. 170

Commentary
This passage is a theological and philosophical argument about the unity of a science. The author begins by stating that the judgment (الحكم) about a science concerns not the subject matter but the composite mental entity (المركب الاعتباري) that is the science itself. The subject matters (المسائل) are its material, and the unity of the science comes from the form (الصورة) which is the aspect of unity (جهة الاتحاد). He then raises an objection: the condition that the subjects of a single science share in a genus or something else does not prevent confusion about unity and difference, because two sciences often share something—at least existence. For example, arithmetic and geometry both study subjects (number and magnitude) that fall under the genus of quantity, yet they are two separate sciences, unlike grammar which studies types of words. The answer is that if the investigation is from the perspective of their commonality (اشتراك), then it is one science; otherwise multiple. The example given is that arithmetic and geometry do not consider time, which is a type of quantity. He cites Ibn Sina's al-Shifa as support, stating that each is a separate science and is called 'consequent' (تالياً) in the sense of being a subsequent division.

موضوع
اتحاد العلم
جنس
كم
حساب
هندسة
نحو
الشفاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Sharing a genus is sufficient for two subjects to belong to a single science.
Correction: Sanusi shows that arithmetic and geometry share the genus of quantity but are separate sciences; unity requires investigation from the aspect of commonality.

Checkpoint

A) Whether they share a genus
B) Whether they investigate from the perspective of commonality
C) Whether they have the same subject matter
D) Whether they use the same method

Key Takeaway
The unity of a science is determined by the perspective from which it investigates its subject matters, not by shared genus or subject matter alone.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
In this lesson, you learned that the unity of a science depends on the perspective of investigation, not just shared subject matter. Think of a subject you study or a skill you practice. Are you guilty of lumping together different disciplines under one label because they share a broad topic? How can you identify the distinct 'aspects of unity' in each to deepen your understanding?

The author describes the science itself as a 'composite mental entity'. When you engage with Islamic knowledge, do you treat the science (like kalam or fiqh) as a mere collection of topics (subject matters), or do you actively seek the unifying form that the great scholars employed? Which approach will you take starting today?
?
The lesson contrasts arithmetic and geometry as separate despite sharing quantity. Where in your life have you assumed that common ground between two ideas or groups makes them identical, when actually their investigating perspectives differ? How can this insight help you avoid confusion in your own learning or in dealing with others?
